P
INTRODUCTION TO THIS EDITION
eople have been asking for a new printing of Best Lessons of a Chess Coach for over a decade. I first went to Sunil and Ed requesting they make just a few minor edits, but we were soon engaged in a major re-write, going back to Ed's original transcriptions of Sunil's classes at Hunter College Elementary School and the National Scholastic Chess Foundation's Saturday chess program at the Girl Scout House in Scarsdale. For this edition, we wanted to be certain that the main theme of each lesson is clear and that we go off on fewer tangents. We also determined where additional or alternate diagrams would benefit the student. By making a slight change in lesson order, we now present a grandmaster game followed by one or more of Sunil's games where he encountered similar positions and concepts. And two additional games, originally planned for the 1993 book but left out due to page count restrictions, have been completed and are presented herein. Another change is in the presentation of the Illustrative Games. They now appear at the end of each thematic section, and include a number of modern grandmaster games. Each of these games is presented with a series of commented diagrams which follow the flow of the game and emphasize the themes. In addition, many of these games are supplemented with exercises. With the great wealth of chess now available on the Internet, something Sunil and Ed did not enjoy in the early 1990s, you can readily find these games and play them through, which we highly recommend. This book is available on the Forward Chess app which also allows you to see all the moves of a game, not just the diagrammed positions. In his lessons, Sunil makes the point that there is validity in studying games in their entirety. Rather than simply solving puzzles, studying games exposes you to many different types of positions and helps you formulate an appropriate strategy to try in your own play. In the time I have been working with Sunil, I cannot count how often people have commented that Best Lessons of a Chess Coach is among their top two or three books because of how much they learned. So, without further ado, this new edition is for them, and for the many younger generations of chess players who would love to have a lesson from one of our nation's most successful chess instructors. Turn the page, class is about to begin. Robert McLellan Editor, Best Lessons of a Chess Coach: Extended Edition 8
O
PREFACE
ne of the biggest stumbling blocks in the development of aspiring chess players comes sometime after they begin to play in tournaments. In the heat of competition, their minds are often plagued with questions they do not know how to answer: "Who has the better position?" "Should I resolve the tension in the center?" "Can I improve the placement of my pieces?" "What is the best way to penetrate my opponent's defenses?" Such questions cannot be answered by mere reference to a laundry list of canned precepts. Every truth about chess must be couched in context - preferably in the context of a real game. To play chess well, and to appreciate it the most, one can do no better than to study complete, well-annotated master games. This book presents a series of classroom lessons by nationally acclaimed chess coach Sunil Weeramantry, aFIDE master who is in current and continual practice. Sunil's lectures bring the reader through the entire life cycle of a game, making clear what is happening at key moments. The cumulative effect of studying these lectures is to give the improving player tools with which to win. We hope the reader will find a good amount of chess entertainment while seeing how the story of each of these games unfolds in the classroom. There is a great deal of beauty, logic, and artistry to admire and enjoy in the games and ideas presented. When Sunil lectures on the games of the great masters, one can imagine being there. When Sunil lectures on his own games, one can share the experience along with him - the excitement, disappointments, and satisfaction of a well-played game. This book is organized into four thematic sections, each containing several lessons. Each lesson presents one complete game that illustrates one or more themes. The lessons are dialogues in which Sunil frames questions for his students. You should pause to answer the questions yourself. Note that the first answer given in the text may not be the correct one. Sometimes these wrong answers provide the best opportunity for learning. Diagrams are provided to help you follow the game and visualize the possibilities, but it is also recommended that you play along on your own chess set. You will also be given exercises within each lecture. You may wish to set up the position on a board, perform your own analysis, and make notes for comparison to the answers given at the end of the lesson. Review notes are provided to reinforce the important points made during each lecture. These notes are segmented into "Themes" and "Advice." As you read these, try to recall the context in which the ideas were presented. If necessary, go back and find the relevant paragraphs. At the end of each of the four thematic sections, you will find Illustrative Games. These games highlight the themes of the associated lessons. Play through these games to get a feel for how thematic positions develop in different situations. Such pattern reinforcement is important to the development of chess skill. Preface If you should encounter chess terms you do not understand, please refer to the Glossary provided at the end of the book.
T
PART A
OUTPOST SQUARES
he games in this section demonstrate the establishment and exploitation of outpost squares. A piece posted on such a square exerts pressure on the opponent's position and supports the attack. d5 is an outpost Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense)
O
LESSON 1
[diagram]
HOME AWAY FROM HOME
ur first lesson introduces the idea of the outpost square as a permanent advantage that can support the steady build-up of an attack. In this game, note how Grandmaster Vasily Smyslov secures his position against counterplay before launching the final assault. This game was played in the USSR Championship in 1945. While Smyslov did not win this event, he went on to place second in the 1948 World Championship tournament and, in 1957, became the seventh World Chess Champion. There are four criteria that qualify a square as an outpost: • It should be a central square, preferably in enemy territory. • It must be safe from attack by a pawn. • It must be subject to effective occupation, preferably by a knight. • It must have an anchor. The anchor should be a pawn. Why is an outpost square important? If you can establish a safe place in enemy territory ("a home away from home"), you can use that square to launch an attack.
V. SMYSLOV - I. RUDAKOVSKY
Moscow 1945
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4
[diagram]
The Open Sicilian. A Sicilian is considered either "open" or "closed" depending on whether or not White plays the move d2-d4, opening both central diagonals within the first three moves. Every player should learn how to play the Open Sicilian. Before playing closed positions, you need the open game experience. At some point, in order to win, a closed position must be opened up.
3...cxd4
Black trades his c-pawn for White's d-pawn; this gives him two center pawns against White's one.
4.Nxd4 Nf6 (D)
Question: There is a trap in this position. Tell me what happens if White plays e5. What is wrong with this move? Answer: It moves a pawn twice. True, but it is not a loss of time, as it places the f6-knight under attack. Look for a tactical reason. Answer: Black has ...Qa5+, checking the king and attacking the e5-pawn. Right. This is adouble attack. Look for one when you see an unprotected piece or pawn.
5.Nc3 d6 6.Be2 Be7 (D)
After 9.f4
[diagram]
Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense) This variation, with black pawns on e6 and d6, is the Scheveningen Sicilian, so-called because it was first played in Scheveningen, asmall village in The Netherlands that hosted a very strong tournament in 1923. While modestly placed in terms of development, the e7-bishop protects the d6-pawn. Not every piece can go on the attack. Black hopes to complete his development, expand on the queenside by pushing his a- and b-pawns, line up his heavy pieces on the c-file, and play for an eventual ...d6-d5, using his extra pawn to strike in the center of the board.
7.O-O O-O 8.Be3 Nc6
Notice that White has placed both bishops on the same central file, leaving the other one open for his rooks. We have now reached the end of the opening. White has all of his minor pieces developed; Black has all but one. (D) Question: How should White further develop his position? Answer: Bb5. No, this is harmless. Black can easily defend with ...Bd7. If White now trades bishop for knight, Black will reply with ...bxc6, getting yet another pawn into the center. The correct move is...
9.f4 (D)
After lO.Wel
[diagram]
After 11...e5 It is extremely important to get two pawns together on the fourth rank whenever feasible. This controls the four squares in front and acts as a screen to provide maneuvering room from behind. Two pawns on the fourth can act as a screen to move pieces around. You do not have to advance these pawns - they perform a valuable function where they are. This move also creates more space for the rook on f1. And it gives you a middlegame plan: White will attack on the kingside. Always try to start an attack on the side of the board where you have more space. Once you establish a space advantage, either the kingside or queenside, move your pieces to that side of the board and conduct an attack. Pay attention to how Smyslov builds up his attack without giving Black counterplay. Comment: What about the weakness on f2? It is not an important weakness if it cannot be exploited. Your king is only as weak as your opponent's ability to attack it.
9...Qc7 10.Qe1 (D)
A key move - Smyslov creates a way for his queen to move to the kingside. You would not have thought of this without the earlier kingside expansion. This is why you must study an opening system; middlegame plans are easier to formulate when you have a thorough grasp of why each opening move was played.
10...Nxd4 11.Bxd4 e5 (D)
After 14>Sxf6 (12,fxe5 variation) After 13...Ac5 (12.fxe5 variation)
[diagram]
Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense) Question: White's bishop is under attack. How should he proceed? Answer: Trade pawns: 12.fxe5 dxe5. Then play 13.Qg3 and pin the e5-pawn. Question: So how might Black respond to the double attack on the pawn? Answer: 13...Bd6. Answer: But then White just wins a free piece with 14.Rxf6. (D) Okay, I see that the g7-pawn is pinned, but is it really a free piece? Exercise 1-1: In the variation 12. fxe5 dxe5 13.Qg3 Bd6, White can try to win a free piece with 14.Rxf6. Does this work? Analyze several moves ahead until all potential checks and captures have been evaluated. Question: We can still conclude that 13...Bd6 is not the best defense; Black has a much stronger move. What is it? Answer: 13...Bc5, creating his own bishop pin, is much stronger. (D) Bravo! If White now plays 14.Qxe5??, Black will play queen takes queen and laugh! Or, Bf14.Bxc5, then 14...Qxc5+ 15.Kh1 Kh8 stopping Rxf6, and Black is fine. Smyslov can see that the tactics with 12.fxe5 do not work, so he adopts a more strategic plan.
12.Be3 (D)
After 12.Ne3
1. Better
2. Better
White chooses to maintain the pawn structure because two connected pawns on the fourth rank enjoy a space advantage over one on the fourth and one on the third. (D) If Black initiates a pawn trade, White retains a pawn on his fourth rank against a black pawn on its third, thereby retaining a space advantage. (D) What White does not want to do is initiate the trade himself, as this will result in an equal pawn position. (D)
3. Equal
6 5 4 3 c d e f After 12,"Ne6?
[diagram]
Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense) You may be wondering, "What does all this have to do with checkmate?" Well, in chess, you fight all sorts of battles, big and small. Winning a game of chess is a matter of accumulating advantages. Do this well and eventually, you will overwhelm your opponent. There is no need to attempt a quick knockout. Black now makes an error that increases White's advantage.
12...Be6? (D)
This loses a valuable tempo and allows White to consolidate his space advantage on the kingside. Current theory recommends 12...Bd7 instead.
13.f5 Bc4
Question: Should Black have offered to trade light-square bishops? Answer: Yes. White's light-square bishop might be able to attack on the kingside. Not at all. It is better to retreat with 13...Bd7. You must distinguish between thinking ahead and merely dreaming! There really is no way for White's e2-bishop to take part in a kingside attack. Do not handicap your thinking by conjuring up threats that cannot be made. Fixed pawn chains restrict the movement of bishops. Here, White's light-square bishop is a bad bishop because its movement is restricted by its own pawns on e4 and f5. Black's light- square bishop is agood bishop because its movement is not hindered by its own pieces. In general, one does not trade the good for the bad. Nevertheless, Black is offering to trade his good bishop for White's bad bishop so that he can get his queen to c4. His idea is to put pressure on e4 and support a pawn break with ...d6-d5. This plan is ill-conceived. After 14."$xc4 After 16.Nd5 (variation)
14.Bxc4 Qxc4 (D)
Question: The contrast in the position is striking. Now who has the good bishop? Answer: White. Question: And how should White protect his e4-pawn? Answer: Qh4. That protects e4, but White must be careful about walking into a discovered attack, for example 15...Nd5. Sometimes the best course of action is to anticipate trouble before it happens. Any other ideas? Answer: Bg5. A very good move! By pinning the knight, this move does two things: itprotects the e-pawn, and it prevents the freeing move ...d5.
15.Bg5 Rfe8
Black protects the pinned piece. Question: What is White thinking about doing next? Answer: He can play Nd5. (D) Not yet. Exercise 1-2: 16.Nd5 is a blunder. Why? After 17,..Bxc2 (variation} Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense)
16.Bxf6
White has identified the potential outpost square on d5, and, even though the bishops will get traded off, he takes the knight on f6 so that he gains absolute control of the d5 square.
16...Bxf6 17.Nd5 (D)
This is a central outpost square. It cannot be attacked by a black pawn and is effectively occupied by a knight which is anchored by the e4-pawn. The knight attacks f6 and e7 on the kingside, and also exerts influence on the queenside. You do not know yet how this game will end, but what you do know is you have seized control of one great square! The knight is like a tennis player in the middle of the court: it can pivot to the right or the left. It will dominate the bad bishop. Of course, White may choose to trade off his knight at the right time, and for the right price. For example, taking the bishop on the next move would weaken Black's castled position. Question: What if Black now plays ...Qxc2 ? (D) Answer: Attack the queen with Rc1. That would cost an extra pawn after ...Qxb2. There is a better move. Exercise 1-3: Find a better move for White after 17...Qxc2.
17...Bd8
After 18x3
[diagram]
After 20...Sc8
[diagram]
This protects c7 and allows the queen to capture the c2-pawn. Smyslov now decides to take care of his queenside weaknesses. He will put a protected pawn on c3, thus stopping Black's counterplay. Then he will switch over to a kingside attack.
18.c3 (D)
Now, if 18...Bb6+, do not even consider trading the knight for the bishop. White simply moves his king to safety. Black's bishop will be stranded on the queenside and will not be able to assist in the king's defense.
18...b5 19.b3
A difficult move to find. This does not weaken c3 because that pawn is defended by the knight in the outpost. By denying Black any counterplay on the queenside, White will be free to launch a kingside attack without distraction.
19...Qc5+ 20.Kh1 Rc8 (D)
Despite occupying ahalf-open file, Black's move does little. Avoid good-looking moves that do nothing for your position. Better is20...f6.
21.Rf3
White plays a rook lift. This maneuver is one of two ways to attack the castled king. The other is a pawn storm. These two techniques do not work together. A rook lift is generally preferred over a pawn storm if both players are castled on the same side of the board. After 22...g6 (variation)
[diagram]
After 22..,gxf6
[diagram]
Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense) Compare the relative strengths of the rooks. Rooks, to be strong, must have a turning point on the file. This is why Black's c8-rook is not as well placed as it looks. White now has a number of favorable elements for a kingside attack: Black's g7 and h7 are targets, and his pieces are out of position. White is shifting his army to the kingside, and his f5- pawn is positioned to disrupt Black's defenses All this is being supported by the knight on the outpost square!
21...Kh8?
Once again, Black should attempt to slow White's attack with 21...f6.
22.f6
This pawn advance would have worked equally well on the previous move. Now, if Black replies with 22...g6 (D), White can initiate a standard middlegame checkmating pattern which forces a win regardless of Black's defense. Learn as many of these patterns as you can, as they will help you steer your attack in the right direction. Exercise 1-4: How should White proceed against 22...g6 ? There are two variations.
22...gxf6 (D)
23.Qh4 Rg8 24.Nxf6 Rg7 25.Rg3 (D)
After 25.sg3 Exercise 1-5: After 25.Rg3, White is threatening a forced mate. What are the moves?
25...Bxf6
Black takes the knight to avoid White's threat.
26.Qxf6
The focal point of the attack has shifted from h7 to g7. It is not uncommon for the focus of the attack to change as the defender begins to protect his position. When there are several weak squares around the king, they provide the attacker with multiple focal points.
26...Rg8
Note that the rook on g3 performs two functions. It attacks g7, and it also defends c3 preventing Black's queen from starting a counterattack.
27.Rd1
White activates his other rook, threatening to capture the pawn on d6 and continue on to the back rank.
27...d5 28.Rxg7 Rxg7 29.Rxd5 1-0
As White's queen protects d8, Black cannot stop White's rook from reaching the eighth rank. After 14.gxf6 (12.fxe5 variation)
[diagram]
After 16,..dxc3 (12.fxe5 variation) Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense)
ANSWERS
Exercise 1-1: In the variation 12.fxe5 dxe5 13.Qg3 Bd6, White can try to win a free piece with
14.Rxf6. Does this work? Analyze several moves ahead until all potential checks and captures
have been evaluated. (D) Answer: White does not win a free piece. Black replies with 14...exd4 15.Qxd6 Qxd6 16.Rxd6 dxc3. (D) Now White should keep the c-file closed with 17.b3. Control of the d-file gives White the advantage. 17.bxc3, on the other hand, destroys White's pawn structure and gives Black excellent counterplay against White's doubled c-pawns after ...Be6 and ...Rac8. Exercise 1-2: 16.Nd5 is a blunder. Why? Answer: 16.Nd5 loses a piece to a frequently seen combination. Black plays 16...Nxd5, protecting his bishop with his knight while simultaneously creating a discovered attack on White's bishop.
(D)
After 16>..Nxd5 (16*Nd5 variation) After 18,Sf2 (17...Bxc2 variation)
[diagram]
After 23.Rh4 (22...g6 variation) The result is the win of a piece for Black regardless of which capture White makes: 17.exd5 Bxg5 or 17.Bxe7 Nxe7. Note that this tactic would work even if the e7-bishop were not protected by the rook. Exercise 1-3: Find a better move for White after 17...Qxc2. Answer: After 17...Qxc2, the right move would be 18.Rf2. (D) This move attacks the queen and protects b2. White wins the exchange without dropping any more pawns: 18...Qc4 19.b3 Qc6 20.Rc1 Qd7 21.Nc7 forking the two rooks. Exercise 1-4: How should White proceed against 22...g6 ? There are two variations. Answer: White can answer 22...g6 with 23.Qh4. (D) After 25.Sg3 Smyslov - Rudakovsky 1945 (Sicilian Defense) The idea isQh6, threatening mate on g7. If 23...Rg8, White wins with the queen sacrifice 24.Qxh7+ Kxh7 25.Rh3#. If instead 23...h5, White still attempts to reach h6 with 24.Qg5. Black's only way of stopping this maneuver is to play 24...Kh7. Now 25.Rh3 sets up a rook sacrifice on h5 with a mating attack. Exercise 1-5: After 25.Rg3, White is threatening a forced mate. What are the moves? (D) Answer: White is threatening 26.Qxh7+ Rxh7 27.Rg8#. This is a typical mating pattern you should familiarize yourself with.
REVIEW
Themes Making a middlegame plan (9.f4 and l0.Qe1). Space advantage in the center (9.f4). Maintaining tension (12.Be3). Bad bishop versus good bishop (12...Be6 and 13...Bc4). The outpost square (15.Bg5, 16.Bxf6, 17.Nd5). Building the attack (10.Qe1; 15.Bg5 through 21.Rf3). Preventing counterplay (18.c3 and 19.b3). Shifting focal points (23.Qh4 through 29.Rxd5). Advice Two pawns on the fourth can act as a screen to move pieces around. Always try to start an attack on the side of the board where you have more space. Your king is only as weak as your opponent's ability to attack it. You must study an opening system; middlegame plans are easier to formulate when you have a thorough grasp of why each opening move was played. Winning a game of chess is a matter of accumulating advantages. Do not handicap your thinking by conjuring up threats that cannot be made. Anticipate trouble before it happens. Avoid good-looking moves that do nothing for your position. A rook lift is generally preferred over a pawn storm if both players are castled on the same side of the board. Rooks, to be strong, must have a turning point on the file. Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian)
I
LESSON 2
[diagram]
KNIGHT MUSIC
n the previous game, we saw the strength of a knight that took up residence on a central outpost square, and eventually lent support to a direct attack on the king. In this game, without even launching a direct attack, a knight posted one rank deeper in enemy territory disrupts White's queenside by totally paralyzing the opposing rooks. This game was played in the U.S. Amateur Team Championship. Over one thousand players compete on 4-person teams in this annual "chess extravaganza," and they all compete in the same division. It is a great tournament, especially for young and improving players like yourselves. The best way to improve your game is to challenge more experienced players.
L. PUGH - S. WEERAMANTRY
Somerset (New Jersey) 1992
1.d4 Nf6 2.c4 g6
This introduces the King's Indian Defense.
3.Nc3 Bg7
Black fianchettoes the king's bishop. Now White has two choices. The most common move is 4.e4, to establish the big center.1 If you are playing Black, itis all right to let White do this so long as you hit back at the right time and not let the center advance on you. My opponent chose to play more conservatively.
4.g3 (D)
After 4,g3
[diagram]
4...O-O 5.Bg2 d6 6.Nf3 Nc6 7.O-O Bg4 (D)
This is the King's Indian Defense Classical Fianchetto, Simagin Variation. It is named after Vladimir Simagin, a Russian grandmaster who was one of the trainers of Vasily Smyslov, the world champion we met in the previous lesson. This move introduces a clear strategy: Black wants to seize control of the central dark squares (d4, e5). Question: How does 7...Bg4 advance Black's plan? Answer: It targets the knight that protects the d4 and e5 squares. Exactly. White's knight on f3 is the principal defender of the central dark squares. Black's purpose in moving to g4 is not just to develop the bishop, but to eliminate this knight. Trading bishop for knight early in the game often saddles the opponent with doubled pawns, as in the Exchange Variation of the Ruy López or in the Nimzo-Indian Defense. This trade, without forcing the creation of doubled pawns, is unusual because strong players typically value bishops over knights. I have played ...Bg4 against players of various strengths. Many of them will chase the bishop away. When they do this, I smile to myself, because it shows that they do not fully appreciate the purpose of the move. My opponent chased the bishop.
8.h3
After 8.d5 (variation) Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) This wastes a move and pushes Black to do exactly what he intended. Do not help your opponent carry out his plans! Question: What is a more constructive move for White? Answer: 8.Be3. That is a solid developing move, but it is too early to decide where to place this bishop. There are other good moves. Answer: d5. (D) Yes. Advance your pawn and chase Black's knight out of the center! This is White's best move, according to current opening theory. Black's knight on c6 does not have a good square to go to. Question: Where could the knight go? Answer: Move back to b8. That may not be so bad at all - back to b8, then out to d7. So long as the center remains closed, this loss of time is not critical. Let's look at some other choices. Answer: Knight to b4. This will be met by a3, forcing the knight to the rim on a6. The way back into the game is through c5, but this will be short-lived because the knight will again be chased by b4. If Black wishes to maintain a knight on c5, his a-pawn should already be on a5. Answer: Knight to a5. This attacks the c4-pawn, but again the knight is on the rim, and Black must be careful lest the knight get trapped. Back to the game...
8...Bxf3 (D)
After 8..Jbtf3 After 10...fixe5 (9.exf3 variation) Question: Which way is White going to take back? Answer: With the bishop, because taking with his f-pawn doubles the pawns and blocks the g2-bishop. It looks like it does not make sense to take with the pawn, but if Black does not find the correct response, itmakes a lot of sense. A long time ago I played this opening, and at the time I did not know it well. I was playing Black against an experienced master who took back with the pawn (9.exf3). I reacted incorrectly and ran into immediate trouble. Iplayed 9...e5 to break up the center. He took my pawn, and I recaptured with the knight (10.dxe5 Nxe5). (D) What I did not appreciate at the time is that Black cannot maintain a knight on e5. White would first protect his c4-pawn with 11.Qe2, then chase the knight away with f4, reopening the long diagonal for his fianchetto bishop. Despite having doubled pawns, White still has good control of the center. Getting stronger at chess is a matter of experience and study. After a loss, go back and study the critical positions. I did so and learned how to respond correctly to the pawn capture. The correct move for Black is 9...d5, threatening to trade pawns and isolate White's d4- pawn. Should White attempt to avoid the trade by playing c4-c5, the d4-pawn will still remain under pressure from the g7-bishop. (D) After 9,..d5 (9,exf3 variation)
[diagram]
After 9...M7 Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) There are two weaknesses associated with an isolated pawn: the pawn itself and the square in front of it, which can serve as an outpost. If an isolated pawn is on its fourth rank or below, it cramps the other pieces and restricts their mobility. If the isolated pawn has advanced to the fifth rank or beyond, and is well protected, it can be strong. It becomes more dangerous as it advances. When playing against an isolated pawn, blockade it, especially with a knight. When playing with an isolated pawn, gain space by advancing it, and create piece play on the adjacent files. In this game, White played the correct way, recapturing with the bishop.
9.Bxf3 Nd7 (D)
Now we have a double attack on the d4-pawn, and it is not easy to protect it. Question: How does White protect the d4-pawn? Answer: Push it. Not this time. Question: Why isd5 no longer a good move? Answer: Black has ...Nd4. Not bad. You are moving to a center square, but you are not choosing the best square for the knight. After 10... ce5 (10.d5 variation] After 10-,.e5
[diagram]
Answer: Move the attacked knight to e5, with a double attack on the c-pawn and the bishop. Right. (D) If White protects the pawn with 11.Qb3, Black can attack it again with 11...Nb6, after which the pawn falls. Exercise 2-1: After 10.d5 Nce5, defending the pawn with 11.Qd4 would be disastrous. Why? In the game, White protected d4 with the e-pawn.
10.e3 e5 (D)
Question: What is the main point of 10...e5 ? Answer: To fight for control of the center. Question: What is Black's best move against 11.dxe5 ? Answer: 11...Ndxe5. Exactly, and again both the c4-pawn and the bishop are attacked. Question: What will Black do against 11.d5 ? Answer: Retreat the knight to e7. Correct. (D) After ll.&eS
[diagram]
Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) Question: Now, with the center closed, how can Black create play? Answer: Pawn to f5. Yes. You must play ...f7-f5 and expand on the kingside. Let me say this again: When a single pawn is on the fourth rank, and there is a chance to get another pawn to an adjacent square, do it!
11.Ne2 (D)
An unexpected reply. White chooses to defend the d-pawn, rather than advancing it and locking the center.
11...f5
This move is normally played when the center is locked. However, it is still effective in this position because White cannot open the center in a meaningful way.
12.c5 (D)
White insists on forcing open the center. What better way to guarantee this than by creating more pawn contact? White hopes his bishop pair will come alive. This is a challenging move. If Kasparov had played 12.c5, I would probably be sitting there shaking! I would think this is some incredible move, just because of the person who played it. Well, you have to forget about who you are playing against and focus on the position at hand. When confronted with a novel or surprising move, take your time. Do not rush your reply. It is always scary to leave the security of the familiar behind you, but a chess player must have the confidence and the courage to strike out on his own. Personally, Ifind the prospect of creating something new to be quite exciting!
12...e4
I decided to block out the bishop with a gain of tempo, and then capture the pawn.
13.Bg2 dxc5 (D)
Question: What does White do now? Answer: Qb3+ recovers the pawn. Yes. This was his plan when he played c5. Of course, I'm not saying it was a good plan.
14.Qb3+ Kh8 15.Qxb7 (D)
After 15.Qxb7
[diagram]
Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) White has succeeded in destroying Black's queenside pawn structure. Black is left with an isolated a-pawn and doubled isolated c-pawns. The c6-knight is also threatened.
15...Nb4
Black takes care of the principal threat. Question: What compensation does Black have for his shattered pawn structure? Answer: Maybe Black can trap White's queen. Good point. You should be alert to such opportunities. Even if the queen does not get trapped, it will lose a lot of time getting back to safety. Look at the placement of White's pieces. There is not a single piece that is beyond the second rank, except the queen. Remember, not even the most powerful piece can successfully tackle an entire army. Answer: The doubled c-pawn can be traded off. Right again. But Black has to be careful about the timing of that trade. If Black allows White's knight to get into d4, his light squares become vulnerable, as he does not have a light- square bishop to protect them. Question: What else does Black have? Answer: The knight can jump into d3. Exactly. Black has obtained active piece play as compensation for the structural weaknesses.
16.Qb5 (D)
After 16.Sb5 Exercise 2-2: After 16.Qb5, Black has a forced win of material. Can you find it?
16...Rb8
This does not win material, but it maintains Black's advantage.
17.Qc4 Nd3 (D)
The knight has found a home! Occupying an outpost square is a goal in itself. Black's knight on d3 neutralizes the d-file and takes away e1 and c1, disrupting White's development. Meanwhile, look at Black's rooks - they have their choice of files. Now, White should not let this knight stay on d3. However, if he tries to get rid of it with Nf4, his pawn structure will be destroyed regardless of which way he recaptures. Another idea for White is to enable Nc1 by playing Rb1 and Bd2. Black can counter by preparing the advance of his c-pawn to c4, strengthening the outpost. Then, if White ever captures the knight, Black will recapture with the c-pawn and establish a monstrous protected passed pawn on d3.
18.Qc2 (D)
After 18,Qc2
[diagram]
Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) White retreats his queen to protect b2. I hope you have noticed that White's last five moves have all been queen moves. What started aggressively (14.Qb3+) has ended rather timidly. Do not move the queen out too early. Even the 14th move can be too early if you have not developed all your pieces. White has nothing to show for the queen's adventures, while Black has gained in development at its expense. As he is not presently under any pressure, Black has time to regroup. The next step, then, is to improve the position of the major pieces. Black's rook on b8 is already in a good position. The queen on d8 and the rook on f8, on the other hand, are not doing anything significant. Question: From a long-term point of view, where should Black's f-rook be placed to help you form a winning plan? Answer: On c6 to support the c5-pawn. He can move the rook there from f6. On the whole, a good plan, but it would be better not to block the c7-pawn. Answer: On d6. Yes, that's very strong. Playing on the d-file works, because d4 is a target. The plan I came up with, however, focused on the c-file. Answer: On c8, then after ...Nb6, Black can push the c7-pawn. Agreed. This idea looks at eliminating the weakness of the doubled isolated c-pawns. With the rook on c8, I can trade the c5-pawn, and then advance the c7-pawn. Question: But first the queen must be moved. Where should it go? Answer: Queen to g5. I considered that, but my play right now is on the queenside. Answer: Queen to e7. Yes. That is the move I played.
18...Qe7
This move helps guard e6 against a possible invasion by White's knight and connects the rooks. It fits in neatly with the plan I have already outlined. After 21.$Jxd4
[diagram]
White now thought for a long time and did what many players are guilty of doing when they cannot find a constructive plan: He pushed a random pawn.
19.a3 (D)
This looks very much like a "wait and see" move. The problem with waiting is that Black has too many options. Question: What new weakness does 19.a3 create in White's position? Answer: The square b3 is weak. Yes. Look at that beautiful square! I was sitting there thinking, "Wow - another perfect outpost!" But first things first. It is time to take over the c-file.
19...Rfc8 20.Rb1
White protects the b2-pawn in order to free the c1-bishop.
20...cxd4 21.Nxd4 (D)
Taking with the knight keeps White's pawn chain intact, but leaves his position horribly cramped. Taking with the pawn might result in its loss, but would at least open a diagonal for the dark-square bishop. Sometimes, it is better not to hang on desperately to every single pawn, but to let one go to secure active play.

ANSWERS
Exercise 2-1: After 10.d5 Nce5, defending the pawn with 11.Qd4 would be disastrous. Why?
(D)
Answer: Black would discover an attack on the queen with 11...Nxf3+ 12.exf3 Bxd4. Exercise 2-2: After 16.Qb5, Black has a forced win of material. Can you find it? Answer: Black could have played to win material with 16...Nc2 17.Rb1 Na3. (D) Forking the rook and queen. Now 18.bxa3 Rb8 19.a4 Rxb5 20.axb5 yields Black a queen for rook and knight, but there is still a way to go for victory. Exercise 2-3: If White tried to pick off a pawn with 24.Qxc4, what would happen next? (D) After 24.Bxc4 (variation)
[diagram]
After 26. xb3 (24,Qxc4 variation)
[diagram]
Pugh - Weeramantry 1992 (King's Indian) Answer: Black would play 24...Nb3, skewering the bishop on c1, which is now attacked three times. The best White can do is bail out with 25.Qxb3 Rxb3 26.Nxb3. (D) White has point-count material equality, but Black is firmly in control of White's cramped position, and can continue to exert pressure with moves like ...Rc2 and ...Qc7, among others.
REVIEW
Themes Contesting the central dark squares (7...Bg4). The strengths and weaknesses of the isolated pawn (9.exf3 d5 variation). Occupying the outpost square as a goal (17...Nd3). Improving piece placement and eliminating weaknesses (discussion after 18.Qc2). Advice Fight back against the pawn center; do not let it advance unopposed. Occupy the square in front of the isolated pawn; it is an excellent outpost. When playing with an isolated pawn, advance it beyond the fourth rank and create piece play on the adjacent file. Avoid trades that increase the activity of your opponent's pieces. Do not send the queen into enemy territory without support. It can get trapped, or lose time getting out. Before undertaking active operations, improve the position of badly placed pieces. Connect your rooks; they work best together. Do not hold onto pawns at all costs; it is better to let one go to secure active play. __________
1 Two pawns in the center is known as the "little center," and three or more is known as the "big
center." (A1) Boleslavsky - Lisitsin 1956

This intermediate move ensures a winning material advantage.

PART B
WEAK SQUARES
he games in this section build upon the ideas of the previous section, with special emphasis on the importance of pawn structure and the role it plays in supporting the attack. They illustrate how exploiting weak squares, whether on one or both colors, allows a player to infiltrate the opponent's position and develop a successful attack. After 2,..d5 (variation) Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined)
A
LESSON 3
[diagram]
HIS MAJESTY ON THE MARCH
common misconception is that advancing a pawn to attack your opponent's piece is always desirable. On the contrary, such an advance may create weak squares in your own position. Every time a pawn advances, it loses control of one or more squares. Once this happens, there is no backing up to try and regain control. In this game, future world champion Alexander Alekhine is playing against Fred Yates, who had already won the British Championship four times and would go on to post two wins against Alekhine. But not this time. Alekhine cleverly exploits the weak squares created by his opponent and uses them to secure a clear path into enemy territory. This game was played in the strong London 1922 tournament, won by Capablanca. Alekhine finished second. A. Alekhine - F.D. Yates London 1922
1.d4 Nf6
A flexible move which retains many options for transposing into different openings.
2.c4 e6
Question:What happens if Black instead tries to play d5 right away? (D) After 4...±e7 After 6... c6 (variation) Answer: White will capture the pawn. Question: Yes, and what happens after Black takes back with Nxd5 ? Answer: e4 attacks the knight and sets up a strong center. Exactly. If Black does not prepare ...d5 with ...e6, he will lose control of the center.
3.Nf3 d5
Black offers to transpose into a Queen's Gambit Declined. Instead, he could try the Queen's Indian Defense with 3...b6. He could also try the Bogo-Indian Defense with 3...Bb4+, a line named after Efim Bogoljubow, an elite player in the first part of the twentieth century.
4.Nc3 Be7 (D)
The transposition into the main variation of the Queen's Gambit Declined is now complete.
5.Bg5 O-O 6.e3
This is the only logical move. White needs to activate his light-square bishop. Now Black has an important decision: should he move his knight to c6 or to d7? Let's look at 6...Nc6 first. (D) Question: If he moves to c6, itblocks the c-pawn. Why is it important for Black to be able to move the c-pawn? What is the word I am looking for? Answer: Space. Yes! Space is extremely important. Do you see how cramped Black's position is when the knight is on c6? After 6*.. bd7
[diagram]
Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined) Question: How many such squares does Black have behind the pawn line to develop his pieces? I want you to identify the squares. You should count the square even if it is already occupied by one of your pieces. Do not count the back rank. Answer: Three - d6, d7, and e7. Yes. Three squares are not enough to place all the minor pieces. Question: Now imagine that Black's c7-pawn is on c5. How many squares would he have? Answer: Five - c6, c7, d6, d7, and e7. Precisely. With a single pawn move, you would almost double your space.
6...Nbd7 (D)
Black chooses the right move. The c-pawn is free to advance as needed. Question: Of course, the bishop on c8 is now blocked. How can you fix this? Answer: Play ...b6 and fianchetto the bishop. Yes, this will also help Black support ...c5. In general, I would say that in queen pawn openings where Black has a central pawn chain (f7/e6/d5), do not place a knight on c6 until the c7-pawn has advanced.
7.Rc1
It is not hard to understand this move - White is positioning his rook for a possible opening of the c-file.
7...c6 (D)
Black decides to advance his c-pawn one square, opting for a solid pawn center. This is the most popular response to Rc1, but there is a drawback. Question: What is the issue with ...c6 ? Answer: It blocks in the bishop. Yes. It is further burying the light-square bishop. If the bishop was bad to begin with, can you see how much worse it is now? Black understands this but feels it is more important to limit White's potential rook activity along the c-file. As already mentioned, Black could fianchetto the bishop instead: 7... b6 8.Bd3 Bb7 9.O-O c5.
(D)
This position offers equal chances. The bishop is definitely more active.
8.Qc2
White doubles up on the c-file.
8...Re8
While there is a possibility that the e-file will open, the real purpose of this move is to free the f8 square. Question: How does Black intend to use this square? Answer: Move the bishop there. After 9,jNd3
[diagram]
Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined) That is possible. Perhaps Black is expecting White to play cxd5, after which he would recapture with the e-pawn and open the file. Question: How else can Black use the f8 square? Answer: He could put the knight there instead. Good.
9.Bd3 (D)
Look at the two batteries White has lined up: Qc2/Bd3 and Rc1/Qc2. See how the queen is coordinating well with the bishop and rook? White is exerting a lot of pressure along both the b1-h7 diagonal and the c-file. White is now ready to castle and his pieces are well coordinated. Black must decide how he is going to complete his development. With pawns on e6 and c6, it does not make sense to fianchetto the c8-bishop.
9...dxc4
Black chooses to trade pawns on c4 to free d5 for his knight. He waits for White's bishop to move to d3 before making this capture, in order to gain a tempo by forcing the bishop to move a second time. However, this comes at a price - Black has to trade his most advanced pawn.
10.Bxc4 Nd5 (D)
After ll...Ra5+ (variation) This sets up a trade of pieces. When in a cramped position, try to trade off pieces to ease the congestion. Question: What should White play in this position and why? Answer: Pawn to e4. It chases the knight out of the center. No. This does not work. Exercise 3-1: If White plays e4, how does Black win material? Question: What else might White play here? Answer: Knight takes knight. No. This trades off two sets of pieces after 11.Nxd5 exd5 12.Bxe7 Qxe7. Although the position is equal, this move actually helps Black achieve his goal of trading pieces, and allows him to re-establish a strong center pawn on d5. Try again. Answer: Knight to e4. Yes, this is what Alekhine plays.
11.Ne4
In Alekhine's own analysis he claims that the more accurate move is 11.Bxe7, as played in Game 11 of the world championship between Capablanca and Emanuel Lasker just a year earlier (Havana, 1921).
11.Ne4 is a new move introduced in this game. Alekhine is taking a calculated risk because
he must have been aware that the clever intermediate move 11...Qa5+ would tilt the balance in Black's favor. (D) This move forces White to move his king to f1. Although he does have several ways to block the check, all variations favor Black. The worst option is to block with Nc3. Exercise 3-2: After 11...Qa5+ 12.Nc3, how does Black win material? Yates did not find the queen check in this game. However, he did learn from his mistake, as when he found himself in this identical position four years later in a game against Grünfeld (Merano 1926), he played 11...Qa5+ and won the game. After 13...b5?
[diagram]
Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined)
11...f5? (D)
Surprised by Alekhine's novelty, Yates goes astray and makes a serious strategic error. Black appears to win a piece by chasing away one of the defenders of the g5-bishop, but this is an illusion. White simply plays the intermediate move 12.Bxe7 and saves his piece.
12.Bxe7 Qxe7 13.Ned2
Far from winning a piece, the move 11...f5 turned e5 into a powerful outpost for White. Question: How does the trade of dark-square bishops affect Black's position? Answer: Black has traded off his good bishop. All his center pawns are fixed on light squares, and the dark squares are now weak. Now it becomes clear why ...f5 is an error. Before advancing a pawn, consider its long- term consequences. Does it justify losing control of critical squares?
13...b5? (D)
Amazingly, Black repeats the same error on the queenside. Once again, he advances a pawn to chase away an enemy piece. Question: What weaknesses are created by this move? Answer: The c6-pawn becomes weak. Yes. Notice how the base of Black's pawn chain has shifted from b7 to c6. This is now a backward pawn on the half-open c-file. Avoid giving yourself a backward pawn on a file that After 15,..a5 abode! gh your opponent controls. Question: Is this the only long-term weakness? Answer: No. The c5 square is another potential outpost for White. Question: How can White take advantage of this? Give me a winning plan. Answer: White can play bishop takes knight. Black must take back with the c-pawn or lose it. Good start. That is one useful trade. Keep going. Answer: Trade the d7-knight, so the f3-knight can occupy e5. Question: How does White do that? Answer: Move the d2-knight to b3, then c5, and trade it for Black's d7-knight. That is totally the right plan. Trading knights enables you to get your f3-knight into e5. It will be a monster!
14.Bxd5 cxd5 15.O-O
White completes his development.
15...a5 (D)
Black hopes to get in ...a5-a4 to prevent White's knight from going to b3.
16.Nb3
White makes it in time.
16...a4 17.Nc5
As you can see, once you formulate agood plan, the moves come easily.
17...Nxc5 (D)
Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined) Black is hoping that trading more pieces will make it easier to hold on for a draw. This is not the case in this position, because simplification increases White's control. Even though avoiding the trade with 17...Nb6 does not solve Black's problems, itwould keep the position closed a little longer. Simplification does not guarantee equality. Question: How does White recapture on c5? Answer: With the queen. Yes. Stick with the plan. You are not trying to create a passed pawn here. You want to maintain control of the file, along with the dark squares and the e5 outpost. After the queen trade, White will double rooks on the c-file. An open file is a highway into enemy territory. When there is only one open file on the board, the player who controls it is in command of the game.
18.Qxc5 Qxc5 19.Rxc5
With fewer pieces on the board, the weakness of the bad bishop becomes even more obvious.
19...b4 20.Rfc1 Ba6 21.Ne5 (D)
White's knight has reached its central outpost and is a dominant force. Despite the open f1- a6 diagonal, Black's bishop has no constructive moves. Exercise 3-3: Should Black play ...Rec8 and attempt to trade rooks? Write down your analysis. After 23.a3 After 25.h4
[diagram]
21...Reb8 22.f3
White initiates the final plan. He will use the weak dark squares to march his king into the center unopposed, adding an extra attacker.
22...b3 23.a3 (D)
White avoids the trade and locks up the queenside pawns. The player who controls the only open file must not allow the opponent to create an open file of his own. Exercise 3-4: Should Black play 23...Rc8 and try to trade rooks now? Write down your analysis.
23...h6 24.Kf2 Kh7 25.h4 (D)
Having locked down the queenside, White turns his attention to the kingside.
25...Rf8
As trading rooks is not an option, Black is reduced to making waiting moves.
26.Kg3
Exercise 3-5: White sticks to his plan, though he could have won material after 25...Rf8. How?
26...Rfb8 27.Rc7
After 30...Ng8
[diagram]
Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined) After you establish control of a file, find the point of invasion. The most effective point will be on the seventh or eighth rank.
27...Bb5 28.R1c5
This puts pressure on the bishop.
28...Ba6 (D)
The bishop retreats. Black cannot let his rook be tied down to its defense; he will need this rook to defend the e6-pawn when it is eventually attacked.
29.R5c6
White attacks the e6-pawn.
29...Re8 30.Kf4
White's king continues its journey.
30...Kg8 (D)
Black moves his king because he is in virtual Zugzwang. One of his rooks is tied down protecting his bishop, while the other must look after the e6-pawn. Any pawn move will result in the loss of material. The only piece that can move with impunity is the bishop, but it does not have an effective move. Question: White now makes a move that further paralyzes Black. What is it? Answer: Nd7. After 34".Eg8 This move will be played, but not yet. Answer: h5. Yes. This further restricts Black's pawn moves and creates another outpost on g6 for the knight.
31.h5 Bf1 32.g3 Ba6 (D)
Question: How does White pursue the attack? Answer: He can double rooks on the seventh rank. That is correct.
33.Rf7 Kh7
Black anticipates White doubling his rooks on the seventh rank, and moves his king to be able to protect the g7-pawn.
34.Rcc7 Rg8 (D)
Black appears to be holding on, as everything is presently defended. White needs a new threat. Question: Can you find the next move? Answer: Nd7, heading for f6. Yes. After 36...ggffi Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined)
35.Nd7 Kh8
Black moves out of the pin along the seventh rank to stop White from playing Nf6+. Question: How should White proceed? Answer: Move the knight to f6 anyway. If Black takes the knight, he will be mated on h7. Very good.
36.Nf6 Rgf8 (D)
Black pins White's rook to the f6-knight, hoping to prevent Rxg7.
37.Rxg7
White sees the mating attack and continues regardless.
37...Rxf6
The only move that stops an immediate checkmate.
38.Ke5
The king delivers the final blow. It certainly helps to have the extra attacker. Black's f6-rook is now lost because if either black rook goes to f8, White delivers mate in two with 39.Rh7+ and
40.Rcg7#. Accordingly, Black resigns. 1-0

Exercise 3-1: If White plays 11.e4, how does Black win material? (D) Answer: Black plays 11...Bxg5 12.Nxg5 (if 12.exd5, then 12...Bxc1 13.Qxc1 exd5+ 14.Be2 Nf6 wins the exchange and a pawn) 12...Qxg5 13.exd5. (D)
13...Qxg2 14.Kd2 (14.Rf1 exd5+)14...Qxf2+ 15.Be2 Qxd4+.
Black is ahead three pawns and stands to win more. Exercise 3-2: After 11...Qa5+ (D) 12.Nc3, how does Black win material? After 24,£jc6 (21...Sec8 variation) Alekhine - Yates 1922 (Queen's Gambit Declined) Answer: If White answers 11...Qa5+ with 12.Nc3? (12.Kf1 is best), then 12...Nxc3 uncovers an attack on the g5-bishop. White cannot play 13.Qxc3 because of 13...Bb4. If instead White tries 13.Bxe7, then Black wins two pawns with the intermediate move
13...Nxa2+. (D)
14.b4 Nxb4 15.Bxb4 Qxb4+.
Intermediate moves are strong tactical resources. You can spot them if you remember that recaptures are not automatic. Note: After 12.Nc3, Black could also have played 12...Nb4, with the follow-up 13.Qa4 Qxa4 14.Nxa4 b5 winning a piece for a pawn. Exercise 3-3: Should Black play 21...Rec8 and attempt to trade rooks? Write down your analysis. Answer: No. If 21...Rec8, then 22.Rxc8+ Rxc8 23.Rxc8+ Bxc8 24.Nc6 (D) wins material as the knight threatens the b4-pawn as well as a knight fork on e7. Exercise 3-4: Should Black play 23...Rc8 and try to trade rooks now? Write down your analysis. Answer: No. If 23...Rc8, then 24.Rxc8+ Rxc8 25.Rxc8+ Bxc8 26.Nd3 Bd7 27.Nc5 (D) After 27. c5 (23...Sc8 variation) wins a pawn as Black's bishop is overloaded. Exercise 3-5: White sticks to his plan, though he could have won material after 25...Rf8. How?
(D)

REVIEW
Themes The struggle for the center (7.cxd5 in the 4...Bb4 variation). The bad bishop (7...c6). Taking control of a potentially open file (8.Qc2). Development to control central squares (8...Re8). Setting up a battery (9.Bd3). Easing a cramped position (9...dxc4). How moving a pawn can create weaknesses in your central structure (11...f5). Finding useful trades (13...b5). Accumulating advantages (13...b5). The advantage of a good plan (17.Nc5). Endgame domination of a bad bishop by a good knight (23...Bxc8 in the 21...Rec8 variation). Sticking to a winning plan (26.Kg3 through to the end). Advice When in a cramped position, try to trade off pieces to ease the congestion. Before advancing a pawn, consider its long-term consequences. Does it justify losing control of critical squares? Avoid giving yourself a backward pawn on a file your opponent controls. Simplification does not guarantee equality. An open file is a highway into enemy territory. When there is only one open file on the board, the player who controls it is in command of the game. The player who controls the only open file must not allow the opponent to create an open file of his own. After you establish control of a file, find the point of invasion. The most effective points are on the seventh or eighth ranks. After Z...g6
P
LESSON 4
[diagram]
A REAL TOGETHER FEELING
awns have many functions, one of which is to provide shelter for one's pieces, particularly the king. A pawn structure that leaves your king unprotected on a set of adjoining squares of the same color, referred to as a weak color complex, can be fatal. To illustrate the danger, I will show you a game I once played in the U.S. Open. This is an unusual game to teach because there are no forcing moves, but it offers an excellent example of exploiting the opponent's errors. Chess players need to be alert as opportunities present themselves.
M. LAMON - S. WEERAMANTRY
Jacksonville (Florida) 1990
1.e4 d6 2.d4 g6 (D)
The Modern Defense, also known as the King's Fianchetto Defense: this belongs to the family of openings known as hypermodern systems, which feature delaying center-pawn moves in favor of developing bishops on the flank (the fianchetto) in order to attack the center from a distance. Notice that the e7- and d6-pawns, by reinforcing dark squares, complement the bishop's control of the a1-h8 long diagonal. As the game progresses, Black will increase this influence by putting a pawn on c5.
3.Nc3 Bg7 4.Be2 (D)
After 4.Ne2 After 5...b5 Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense) This is a good move, a flexible move. It is wise not to telegraph your intentions to your opponent. With this move order, White can direct his play in a number of ways: he can bring his knight to f3 (the Classical System), set up a broad pawn center with f2-f4 (the Pseudo-Austrian Attack), or start a flank attack with h2-h4-h5 or even g2-g4 (the Bayonet Attack).
4...c6
Black delays the development of his king's knight in favor of queenside play. This is a hallmark of the Modern Defense.
5.Nf3 b5 (D)
Black goes for immediate queenside expansion to counter White's space advantage in the center. Prior to this move, White's pieces occupied four ranks while Black's only occupied three.
6.a3 a6
Five pawn moves in the first six moves of the game! Should Black not develop some pieces instead? Well, in this opening, you stake out your territory first, then bring your pieces out later. The point of ...a7-a6 is to strengthen the pawn structure in order to prepare the subsequent advance of the c-pawn. The move ...c6-c5 will challenge more central dark squares and increase the influence of Black's dark-square bishop. Naturally, an immediate ...c6-c5 fails because the pawn on b5 would be left unprotected.
7.O-O Nd7 8.h3 Qc7
Black's last two moves provide further support for ...c6-c5.
9.Be3 Ngf6 10.Qd2 O-O (D)
White has moved all of his pieces off the back rank except for his king and two rooks. Development is not complete until the rooks are connected. Question: What must Black do to complete his development? Answer: Move out his light-square bishop. Question: Where should the bishop go? Answer: Put it on b7 to control the other long diagonal. Yes. This fits in with the hypermodern strategy.
11.Rfd1 Bb7 (D)
Each player has now completed his development. This is where chess becomes difficult. Black's bishops are both fianchettoed, and they are on the outside attacking in. White's bishops are in the center, on the inside attacking out. There is no contact between the two armies at the moment, and every piece is still on the board. Question: Whose bishops are stronger? Answer: Black's. They are attacking the whole center area. Excellent.
12.Ne1
Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense) White moves his knight out of the way, hoping to expand on the kingside with f2-f4.
12...c5 (D)
Black makes pawn contact first. When all the pieces are still on the board, look for a pawn trade to open lines. Comment: Why did you break with ...c5 ? Why not with ...e5 ?
12...e5 helps White. He can capture on e5 forcing Black to recapture with his d6-pawn. This
will bury the g7-bishop, and surrender control of the d-file to White's heavy artillery. 12...c5, on the other hand, allows Black to recapture with the knight, keeping the long diagonal open for the dark-square bishop and retaining a better pawn structure. Question: After 12...c5, is Black threatening anything? Answer: The e4-pawn is threatened. Yes, a very serious threat. White can stop this attack by playing d5, but the presence of pawns on c5 and b5 creates dynamic attacking chances for Black on the queenside. Nevertheless, d5 was certainly better than what was played.
13.f3? (D)
Question: White protects e4 with his f-pawn. What is wrong with this strategy? Answer: It prevents the knight from coming back to f3. He wanted to play f4, then bring the knight back. Now he can't do that. After 13...c4 (variation) Good point. He appears to have given up on the f4 idea. But f3 is bad because it restricts the mobility of both the knight and the bishop. Question: There is another serious weakness with f3. What is it? Answer: The dark squares are weak. Very good. Some people look at a chessboard and see 64 squares, while others look at a chessboard and see 32 dark squares and 32 light squares. If you are strong enough to look at a position like this and say, "Aha, f3 is bad because it weakens the dark squares around the king," then you understand the position clearly. All the kingside pawns are on light squares, so Black will try to attack the weak color complex. Question: Black could now play 13...c4 (D) and keep White's knight out of d3. Is that a good idea? Answer: Yes. It stops the knight from moving in, and also shuts out the bishop on e2. The knight on c3 cannot move either. If it goes to d5, White will lose a pawn. Well, it does take several moves away from White. Question: Now give me a reason why Black would not play ...c4 ? Answer: It weakens the dark squares on the queenside. True. I guess you'll never forget color weaknesses again! I must, however, point out that a weak color complex presents greater danger when it is in the vicinity of the king. Answer: You are giving up some control in the center. That's the right idea! ...c4 is a bad move because Black can no longer force open the center. The reason you need to open the center is to activate the bishops. As we discussed, Black's bishops are stronger because they have potential targets. Advancing ...c4 closes the position, taking away these targets. What should happen is the center must be destroyed. Trade the pawns off and free the bishops. The position then has much more potential than it would have with ...c4. You really cannot let the two white pawns on d4 and e4 continue to sit there. If you do, then the entire hypermodern strategy of playing against the center with fianchettoed bishops is ruined. Not trading pawns now would be a serious strategic error.
13...cxd4 14.Bxd4 (D)
Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense) Question: The opening that started off as a Modern has changed character. What does it now resemble? It is an opening that most players are familiar with. Answer: The Sicilian Dragon. This happens quite often. You take an opening system, play it in a certain move order and transpose into an entirely different system. The c5- and d4-pawns have been traded, and now we have a Sicilian Dragon. Normally, these pawns are traded on the third move. This time it is happening ten moves later. Question: If you compare this to a normal Sicilian Dragon, Black's pawn structure is the same, but some of his pieces are on different squares. Which ones? Answer: The knight on d7 and the bishop on b7. Question: What advantage does Black enjoy that he would not normally have? Answer: Black has more space on the queenside. Yes. This is something that Black prays for in the Dragon! Answer: And his light-square bishop is very active. Very good. Question: What is wrong with White's position? Answer: White looks cramped. The light-square bishop and the knight on e1 are both useless. Question: Where is that knight normally placed? Answer: On d4. Yes, that knight is normally on d4! Question: In that case, where should the dark-square bishop be? Answer: On e3. Correct. This bishop should be behind the knight so that it is less vulnerable to attack. Question: If the bishop on d4 is traded, what are the consequences for White? Answer: He will have trouble protecting his dark squares. After 15.b3
[diagram]
Yes. White will get killed on the dark squares! This sets the course for the rest of the game. Black will go after this bishop, trade it off, and infiltrate on the dark squares.
14...Nb6
Clearly, no good player would trade this bishop for a knight. Black is planning to play ...Nc4 and annoy White's queen. White decides not to let this happen.
15.b3? (D)
When my opponent made this move, I nearly fell out of my chair! This is such a short- sighted move. White is only thinking one move ahead. This does stop ...Nc4, but the long-term consequences are considerable. Question: This move creates weaknesses that will not go away. What are they? Answer: The bishop on g7 is much stronger now. True. That bishop would be a monster if White did not have his own bishop on d4, as it would bear down on the diagonal unopposed and exploit the pinned knight on c3. Answer: You can set up a queen-rook battery along the c-file, and get more pressure on c3. Excellent. The c3 square is where the c-file intersects with the long diagonal. It is therefore a critical square. Removing its protection by advancing the b-pawn weakens both the file and the diagonal. The knight coming into c4 would have been annoying, but weakening c3 is much worse. This kind of mistake is common. Do not sacrifice long-term interests for short-term gain. Black is winning, but there is still some work to do.
15...Rac8 16.Rac1 e5 (D)
After 18,&hl
[diagram]
Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense)
17.Bxb6?
This is the trade I was hoping for, but he need not have made it. Exercise 4-1: How could White avoid the trade 17.Bxb6 and not lose a piece?
17...Qxb6+ 18.Kh1 (D)
Now we come to the final phase of the game. Look at White's pieces - they are all huddled together. Contrast that with the mobility of Black's pieces. Rather than interfering with each other's movements, their freedom enhances their power. There's an interesting exercise to help you determine who controls more space. Count the number of squares your pieces are attacking in your opponent's half of the board, and compare it to the number of squares he is attacking in your half. We can count a square as many times as it is being attacked. A possible capture also counts as a square. Let's try it. Exercise 4-2: Using the position after 18.Kh1, determine who controls more space. Doing this exercise gives you a sense of mobility. The more squares you can access in your opponent's territory, the more attacking chances you will enjoy. Now, how does Black proceed? How does he penetrate White's position? I will tell you what a lot of players would do here. They would play ...Qf2, believing that the queen is accomplishing After a b c a e f g h something by penetrating into the heart of White's position. But, what is the queen threatening from f2 that it is not already doing from b6? Nothing. The best time to bring in the power pieces is when there are targets to attack. Question: So, what moves can Black play to realize his attacking potential? Answer: ...Nh5. Yes, avery good idea. The knight can then come in on f4 or g3. Notice how the queen and knight own these dark squares since White no longer has a dark-square bishop. Question: Black could use more firepower. Which other pieces could he activate? Answer: The rooks. Move one to c7 and double them up. This is a good idea, but the rooks are too far away from the action. Answer: The bishop on g7. That's the idea! This is crucial. You must understand the importance of getting Black's dark- square bishop into the game, even if that appears difficult to do right now. Think in terms of what you want to accomplish in a position. First focus on your overall strategy, then on specific moves.
18...Nh5 19.Kh2
White is worried about the knight's jumping into g3.
19...Nf4 (D)
Question: What is the value of bringing this knight into f4? Answer: To play ...Qf2, attacking g2. It is still too soon to bring the queen in. Besides, g2 is already protected. Answer: To attack the bishop on e2. I did not move the knight here to capture his bishop. Sometimes you may have friends in the enemy camp. At the moment, the bishop on e2 is my greatest friend - it gets in White's way. Right now, you could not pay me to take that bishop! Do not trade active pieces for inactive pieces. Question: What did we say Black is trying to do? Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense) Answer: Get the bishop on g7 into the game. Question: Now make the connection: why ...Nf4 ? Answer: To bring the bishop to h6. Of course! We are setting up a screen so the bishop can move safely to h6. Look at which of White's pieces are on the c1-h6 diagonal. They make great targets.
20.Bf1 Bh6 21.Nd5 (D)
Question: White's last move simply delays the inevitable. How does Black reply? Answer: Bishop takes knight. Question: Excellent!...Bxd5 is absolutely the best move in the position. Why? Answer: It removes the centralized knight. Black's light-square bishop isn't doing much. Right! This bishop is not participating in the attack, so use him to remove the troublesome knight. We just said, "Do not trade active pieces for inactive pieces." The flip side is that you want to trade inactive pieces for active ones.
21...Bxd5 22.exd5 (D)
Everything appears in place for the attack, but there is one final preparation to be made. Question: What is the final piece to this puzzle? Answer: The bishop on h6 must be protected so the knight can set up a discovered attack. Question: So, what should Black play? Answer: ...Kg7. Now you are talking chess! Look how easy it is. Black wants to move his knight, so what does he do? He plays this quiet little king move which turns out to be the best attacking move in the entire game. Even the king has joined in the attack!
22...Kg7! 23.Rb1 (D)
White moves one of his major pieces off the c1-h6 diagonal, but it is too late. Question: Now we are ready for the final assault. What is the best move? Answer: ...Nxh3, with a discovered attack on White's queen. Correct.
23...Nxh3 24.Qd3 Qg1+
Black is not afraid to sacrifice the knight.
25.Kxh3 Bf4 (D)
This creates a mating net. If the opponent has an escape route, do not keep checking. Seal off the escape route first. Unable to avoid mate, White resigns. 0-1 Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense) Just a glance at the final diagram should make it clear that Black's control of the dark squares around White's king was the critical factor in the game. After 18.Qhl
[diagram]
ANSWERS
Exercise 4-1: How could White avoid the trade 17.Bxb6 and not lose a piece? Answer: After 16...e5 (D), Black threatens the bishop on d4, which protects the knight on c3. In the game, White played
17.Bxb6 to avoid losing a piece. He could have done so without making this trade.
White just retreats the bishop to e3. After 17.Be3 Qxc3 18.Qxc3 Rxc3, the b6-knight is no longer protected, so White restores material equality with 19.Bxb6, retaining the protector of the dark squares. Exercise 4-2: Using the position after 18.Kh1 (D), determine who controls more space. Answer: For each side, we count the squares attacked in the opponent's half of the board. We count d4 twice, as it is attacked twice. So we have thirteen attacks by Black, and nine by White. That's a big difference. Black's attacking chances are better. Lamon - Weeramantry 1990 (Modern Defense)

Themes Hypermodern systems (comments on 2...g6). Connecting rooks on the back rank (8...Qc7; 10.Qd2). Attacking the center with fianchettoed bishops (11...Bb7; comment on 2...g6). Weak color complexes (introduction; 13.f3). Opening the center (13...c4 variation; 13...cxd4). Opening system transpositions (comments after 14.Bxd4). Trading to weaken squares of a color (discussion after 14.Bxd4). Long-term weaknesses (introduction; 13.f3; 15.b3). Assessing how much space you control (after 18.Kh1). Trading inactive pieces for active ones (21...Bxd5). Constructing a mating net (25...Bf4). Advice Development is not complete until the rooks are connected. Do not sacrifice long-term interests for short-term gain. The best time to bring in the power pieces is when there are targets to attack. First focus on your overall strategy, then on specific moves. LESSON 5
