ONE TO REMEMBER
am going to show a game I played during an event held in England back in 1972. This is another illustration of how to exploit aweak color complex. In the game featured in our previous lesson, Iwas able to take advantage of a weakness my opponent created. This time I had to induce the weakness. To do this, I sacrificed a rook to decoy the dark-square bishop, which is the natural protector of the weakened squares. Then I built an attack which delivered victory.
R. HARRIS - S. WEERAMANTRY
London, 1972
1.e4 d6 2.d4 Nf6 3.Nc3 g6
This introduces the Pirc Defense, popularized by the Yugoslav grandmaster Vasja Pirc. The essential difference between the Modern Defense and the Pirc is that in the former, Black fianchettoes his king's bishop before developing the knight.
4.f4 (D)
White chooses the Austrian Attack, an aggressive response to the Pirc. He is following classical principles of development by setting up a broad pawn center, while Black uses the hypermodern concept of challenging the center from afar. Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack) I happen to like playing against the broad center. King's Fianchetto players get energized when they see a wall of pawns that they can target. True, the center is very strong, but so is the fianchetto. This is like two gladiators of old fighting with different weapons - one with a sword and shield, the other with a net and trident.
4...Bg7 5.Nf3 O-O (D)
There is an important alternative here. Black can hit back at the center immediately with
5...c5. This move was made popular after it was employed by Bobby Fischer in his celebrated
1972 World Championship match against Boris Spassky. It led to a hard-fought draw.
Question: After ...O-O, White has a choice of how to proceed. What moves can he play? Answer: f5. Notice that f5 would lose a pawn. Some people may argue that it is worth a pawn to break up Black's fianchetto structure. I disagree. Black can take the pawn safely with 6...gxf5 7.exf5 Bxf5 and retreat the bishop, ifnecessary, to g6 to strengthen the castled position. If White wants to play f5, the proper way to do it is to play Bd3 first to give f5 more support. Answer: e5. (D) Now, there is an interesting move. In fact, I have played this position from both sides. Black can trade pawns immediately with 6...dxe5, but this leads to a space advantage for White after 7.dxe5 Qxd1+ 8.Kxd1. The balanced pawn structure makes it difficult for Black to After 7,h4 (6.e5 variation)
[diagram]
After 5...O-O counter White's control of the center. The fact that White's king has moved does not present any danger, as queens have been traded and Black is not fully developed. The other option is for Black to accept the challenge with 6...Nfd7 7.h4. (D) Question: What is the best way to answer an attack on the flank? Answer: With a counterattack in the center. Right! The most effective way to handle a flank attack is to strike back in the center. Do you understand why? The attacker must have unimpeded movement of his pieces from one sector of the board to the other in order to reinforce the attack. If the enemy breaks through the middle, he will break the coordination of the attacking pieces. Question: Now that you understand the idea, what should Black play? Answer: ...c5. Yes. This is a very clever move. You are attacking the base of White's pawn chain and placing pawns in contact at two different points. If White takes the c5-pawn, Black takes the e5-pawn. If White takes the d6-pawn, Black takes the d4-pawn. If White does not take either, then Black takes both! First take the d4-pawn, then the e5-pawn. If both of White's pawns disappear, so does his center. To break a pawn chain, undermine the base and simultaneously put pressure at other points along the chain. Back to the game after 5...O-O: (D) After 7...Ka5 (6.±e2 variation) After 6,Jld3 Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack) Question: So far, we have examined pawn advances. What else should we consider? Answer: Be2. Yes, it is time to consider some piece moves. Black now strikes at the center with 6...c5. White's best response is 7.dxc5. In this position, Black should not recapture, as moving his d6- pawn will allow White to expand in the center. Question: If Black will not recapture with 7...dxc5, how can he justify giving up a pawn? Answer: 7...Qa5 (D) pins the knight on c3 and threatens the center pawn. Excellent. 7...Qa5 is a very powerful move. Once White protects e4, Black has time to capture on c5 with the queen and maintain his d6-pawn. Most inexperienced players will respond by capturing the entire black pawn chain, continuing with 8.cxd6 Nxe4 9.dxe7 Re8. This, however, works out very well for Black. White's king is still in the center, the attack on the pinned knight on c3 is very strong, and once Black opens up the center, the influence of the fianchettoed bishop increases. Best for White after
7...Qa5 is8.O-O Qxc5+ 9.Kh1.
There is one other bishop move White might try. Answer: Bd3. By process of elimination, we have arrived at the move that was played in this game.
6.Bd3 (D)
After 7.O-O
[diagram]
Question: We have seen how 6...c5 is a good idea in response to 6.Be2. Does the same idea work against 6.Bd3 ? Answer: No, because the e4-pawn is now protected for a second time. Bravo! Once you start making these connections, you are on your way to becoming a better player. You must be able to determine if an idea is still applicable when the position has changed. Learn to appreciate subtle differences. Question: How does Black continue his development after Bd3 ? Answer: Black can play ...c6 followed by ...b5. There is a drawback to this plan. Queenside expansion is too slow. It allows White uncontested play in the center. I played...
6...Nc6
There have been more recent developments in this line, in particular 6...Na6. You may wish to examine them yourself. The point behind 6... Nc6 is to put pressure on the central dark squares, and to prepare a pawn break on e5.
7.O-O (D)
Question: What should Black play next? Answer: ...e5. Good idea. This is consistent with the idea of striking in the center.
7...Bg4
Black pursues the same idea in a different way. Pinning the knight threatens the pawn on d4.
8.Be3
White protects d4 and completes his development at the same time. Another good option is
8.e5, which appears to give White a slight edge.
Question: Now, what must Black do, before it is too late? Answer: Push the e-pawn. Push the pawn and hit the center before White plays to e5 himself! Notice how Black's last two moves (6...Nc6 and 7...Bg4) each support ...e7-e5. The knight move adds a defender, and After 8"*e5 After 10,,.£jxe5 (10,fxe5 variation) Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack) the bishop move subtracts an attacker. You can already see that the battle will be over who controls the dark squares.
8...e5 (D)
A good way to break up a three-pawn center is to push the pawn that is on the same file as the middle pawn in your opponent's formation. In this way, you are attacking each of his outside pawns, setting up two trading options to break his structure. Should the opponent initiate the trade, he should capture from one side and then advance the pawn on the other side. He should not capture twice so as to ensure he is not left with an isolated pawn. Look at what happens to his center if he captures twice after 9.dxe5 dxe5: 10.fxe5 Nxe5: (D) His once-proud center is reduced to rubble. Furthermore, Black's knight on e5 is very strong because it occupies the square in front of the isolated pawn.
9.dxe5 dxe5 10.h3
White took once, but did not push the other pawn. Instead, he attacked the bishop.
10...Bxf3 (D)
After 10...±xf3 After 12, c5
[diagram]
This is not the most accurate move order. Black should have played 10...exf4 isolating White's e4-pawn. The best play would then be 11.Bxf4 Be6, with play for both sides. Note that if White first played 11.Bc5, we would transpose back into this game with 11...Bxf3 12.Qxf3 Nh5.
11.Qxf3 exf4 12.Bc5 (D)
White hopes to chase Black's rook out of f8 in order to create pressure along the f-file and particularly on f7.
12...Nh5
Surprise! Realizing that the dark squares around White's king are weak, Black protects his f4- pawn and offers to give up the exchange in order to eliminate White's key defender of those dark squares.
13.Bxf8 (D)
Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack) This identical position was reached by transposition in the 1988 correspondence game N. Carlsson-M. Carlsson. In that game, Black recaptured on f8 immediately. Ichose to delay the recapture in favor of two intermediate moves that bring two black pieces into attacking position. Question: What is the first of these intermediate moves? Answer: ...Bd4+. Correct. This brings Black's bishop to a critical diagonal.
13...Bd4+ 14.Kh2 (D)
White moves his king to h2, as 14.Kh1 would allow Black's knight into g3 with check. Question: Black still does not have all of his pieces in the best attacking positions. More preparation is required. What is the next intermediate move? Answer: ...Qh4. ...Qh4 could be an important part of the attack, but, ifplayed immediately, White would try to force a trade of queens with Qg4. As a general principle, when you are defending, trade queens; when attacking, keep them on. Answer: ...Ne5. Yes. The c6-knight now joins the attack.
14...Ne5 (D)
After 16dLe7! (variation) Look at what has happened - in just a few moves, Black's pieces have taken total control of the dark squares. We could make a movie out of this - Invasion of the Dark Squares. Notice where White's dark-square bishop is while all this is going on - conveniently out of the picture. White's queen must move. If it goes to e2, Black plays ...Ng3, and the other knight comes dancing in with tempo!
15.Qd1
Black is down a rook at present. Does he have enough compensation? I spent a good half- hour trying to decide whether to take the bishop or keep on attacking. Ifelt that the attack had enough momentum and did not want to waste a tempo recapturing the bishop.
15...Qh4
Computer analysis suggests that 15...Kxf8 is stronger. Now White gets greedy and tries to hang on to all his material. Instead, he could have played the remarkably annoying 16.Be7! (D), which was pointed out in annotations to the correspondence game referred to earlier. White attempts to draw Black's queen away from its attacking position. After 16...Qxe7, White will gain time to reorganize his defense with 17.Be2. Though Black still has the advantage, the crisis is forestalled. When ahead in material, do not hesitate to give back all or part of it as needed. In the game, White did not find 16.Be7, but instead played After 16.1h6?
[diagram]
Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack)
16.Bh6? (D)
Now White has serious problems. See how strong the f4-pawn is? It is controlling g3, a key square. The bishop on d4 is patrolling the g1-h7 diagonal, preventing White's king from moving back there. The two knights are ready to jump into the fray. All this highlights Black's complete control of the dark squares around White's king. Meanwhile, what is White doing? The bishop on d3 isnothing but an overgrown pawn. The rook on f1 has little mobility. The bishop on h6 is still out of the game. White has no influence over the dark squares at all. When all the pawns around the enemy king are on the same color, the opposite color squares become weak. Attack on the weak color. Question: Everything is ready for the final attack. What does Black do? Answer: Play ...f5; then the knight can come to g4 with check. Your idea of protecting g4 to set up ...Ng4+ is good. However, White can simply take that pawn and refute your plan. Show me another way to get in ...Ng4+. Answer: Black can play ...f3. Excellent! This is an interference move. It interferes with the queen's protection of g4 and attempts to destroy the pawn cover in front of White's king.
16...f3! (D)
After 19,Sxg3 Black's leapfrogging knights now threaten ...Ng4+ followed by ...Ng3#. Exercise 5-1: If White plays 17.gxf3, show how Black wins.
17.Rxf3
White is still up a rook, but with Black's pieces swarming around his king, it certainly does not appear that way. Inactive or underdeveloped pieces have a way of blending inconspicuously into the background, while active pieces seem to jump right at you! Question: What should Black play now? Answer: ...Ng4+. Yes, taking the rook would be a mistake. Black's knights are much stronger.
17...Ng4+ 18.Kh1 Ng3+ 19.Rxg3 (D)
White has no choice but to take the knight. Question: What does Black do now? Answer: ...Qxg3. No. That is precisely what White is wishing for; he can then continue with Qxg4. Black has something much better. Answer: ...Nf2+ forking the king and the queen. Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack) That's it. Here I was feeling quite satisfied, but White has one final trick left.
19...Nf2+ 20.Kh2 Nxd1 21.Bg5 (D)
Question: White moves his bishop out of danger and gains a tempo by attacking Black's queen. Does the queen have a safe move? Answer: No. h5 is the only square not being attacked, but 22.Be2 would trap the queen. Indeed. White thought he had caught me. It would have been a shame to ruin the game, when I was on the brink of victory, by making a careless move. Question: So, what is the winning move? Answer: ...Bg1+. Excellent. This is an intermediate move that draws the king away from the rook. It is important to not always just respond to an attack, but to look for counterattacks. My opponent's face fell a mile when he saw this!
21...Bg1+ 22.Kxg1 Qxg3 23.Rxd1 Qxg5 (D)
White resigns. 0-1 Less-experienced players should not resign at this stage; there is still some play left. However, we are both strong masters, and evidently my opponent felt that he had no real chances. White's bishop and knight are powerless against the queen. Even if White sets up a fortress, there will come a point when something will have to move and the fortress will crumble. After IZgxfj (variation)
[diagram]
ANSWERS
Exercise 5-1: If White plays 17.gxf3 (D), show how Black wins. Answer: 17...Qg3+ 18.Kh1 Qxh3#. Harris - Weeramantry 1972 (Pirc Austrian Attack)
REVIEW
Themes Weak color complexes (introduction). Broad pawn center versus the fianchetto (4.f4). A flank attack versus counterattack in the center (7.h4 variation). Breaking a pawn chain (7.h4 c5 variation). Attacking the central dark squares (8...e5). Intermediate moves to improve piece position (13...Bd4+; 14...Ne5). Giving back material to slow an attack (16.Be7 variation). Interference move (16...f3). Advice The most effective way to destroy a flank attack is with a counterattack in the center. To break a pawn chain, undermine the base and simultaneously put pressure at other points along the chain. You must be able to determine if an idea is still applicable when the position has changed. Learn to appreciate subtle differences. As a general principle, when you are defending, trade queens; when attacking, keep them on. When ahead in material, do not hesitate to give back all or part of it to stave off disaster. When all the pawns around the enemy king are on the same color, the opposite-color squares become weak. Attack on the opposite color. After 22...g6
ILLUSTRATIVE GAMES B
Weak Squares (B1) Tarrasch - Réti, Vienna 1922 [B10]
1.e4 c6 2.Nc3 d5 3.Nf3 Nf6 4.exd5 cxd5 5.d4 Bg4 6.h3 Bxf3 7.Qxf3 e6 8.Bd3 Nc6
9.Be3 Be7 10.O-O O-O 11.a3 a6 12.Ne2 b5 13.Bf4 Qb6 14.c3 Na5 15.Rad1 Nc4 16.Bc1
Qc6 17.Ng3 a5 18.Rfe1 b4 19.axb4 axb4 20.Nf5 exf5 21.Rxe7 bxc3 22.bxc3 g6 (D) In his desire to protect the f5-pawn, Black creates dark-square weaknesses that White will be able to exploit. Instead 22...Nd6 offers counterplay without weakening the kingside. For instance, 23.Bxf5 Nxf5 24.Qxf5 Ra1 creates a dynamically balanced position.
23.Bh6 Nb2 24.Rb1 Nxd3 25.Qxd3
In addition to his dark-square play, White enjoys a strategic advantage by virtue of his two- to-one queenside majority, even if that majority is temporarily restrained. Any future trade of pawns there will give White a passed d-pawn.
25...Rfb8 26.Rxb8+ Rxb8 27.Qg3 Rd8 28.Qe5 Ra8 29.Rc7 Qe6
Black offers to trade queens in order to eliminate the danger of a kingside attack by White.
30.Qxe6 fxe6 31.Rg7+ Kh8 (D)
(B2) Alekhine - Yates 1923 Although there is no longer any danger of a mating attack, Black is extremely vulnerable on the dark squares. With all his pawns fixed on light squares, there is little Black can do to contain White's dark-square bishop.
32.Re7 Kg8 33.f3

21.b4 Qb5 (D)
The only way for Black to avoid losing a piece is to pin the b4-pawn. However, both Black's queen and his dark-square bishop have now drifted to the wrong side of the board, leaving Black's king defenseless.
22.Qg3
As Black's key defenders are now out of position, White choses to apply pressure on the dark squares. An alternative approach is to open the kingside with 22.f5.
22...Bb6
Black need not move his bishop back immediately as White's b4-pawn is still pinned. However, he does not have a good alternative. For instance, if Black attempts to protect his g7- pawn with 22...f5, White will plant his queen on g6 and Black will be unable to chase it out of this outpost.
23.Nd7
White threatens mate on g7. The attack on the dark squares has begun.

[diagram]
25.Be5
White continues his offensive on the dark squares. He could, however, achieve the same goal with even greater effect by advancing his f-pawn to f5, opening the h2-b8 diagonal for his queen.
25...Rcc8 26.Qh4 h5

(B12) Graif - Can, Chicago 2019 [B42] This game was played by one of Sunil's longtime students, William Graif, who has been a national scholastic champion twice in the United States and twice in Canada. Even though he was on the losing end, William sent this game to Sunil as an example of enterprising play against a weak color complex.
1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 e6 3.d4 cxd4 4.Nxd4 a6 5.Nc3 Qc7 6.Bd3 Nf6 7.Qe2 d6 8.O-O Nbd7
9.a4 b6 10.f4 Be7 11.Bd2 O-O 12.Rae1 g6 13.Bc4 Bb7 14.Ba2 Rfe8 (D)
After 2O.Bf3? (B12) Graif - Ali Maranda 2019 Black has adopted a "Hedgehog" formation where none of his pawns have advanced beyond the third rank. Despite the relative lack of space, Black's position is very solid and there are no clear targets for White to attack. Black's last move anticipates White's pawn break with f4-f5, and places the f8-rook on the e- file as a deterrent. It also frees the f8 square to enable the transfer of his bishop to g7.
15.f5 exf5 16.exf5 d5
Black shuts down the a2-g8 diagonal and gives his dark-square bishop other options for deployment.
17.fxg6 hxg6
The trade of pawns created opportunities for both sides. White's rooks are well placed on the e- and f-files and he has pressure on Black's isolated d-pawn. Black has play on the dark squares.
18.Bf4 Qc5 19.Be3 Bd6
Black chooses the sharpest continuation, leaving his queen in a potential discovered attack. His bishop, for the moment, is in a far better spot than it would be on g7, and is eyeing the h2- pawn.
20.Qf3? (D)
White moves his queen out of the pin but misses Black's threat. To keep the balance, White must play 20.h3. After 25.£rf5
[diagram]
20...Rxe3!
This powerful exchange sacrifice eliminates the key defender of White's dark squares and sets up a winning attack.
21.Qxe3 Ng4 22.Qd2 Bxh2+ 23.Kh1 Bg3 24.Re2 Nde5
Black has played a series of forcing moves since the exchange sacrifice and completely dominates the dark squares around White's king. Not only does 24...Nde5 move the second knight into attacking position, it also shields the e7 square, enabling Black's queen to relocate to the kingside.
25.Nf5 (D)
White must rely on drastic measures to survive, and sacrifices a knight in search of counterplay. The alternative 25.Nf3 is met by 25...Kg7 threatening ...Rh8+, after which Black will win material.
25...gxf5 26.Qg5+ Kf8
While this move is strong enough to win, Black also has a most unusual way of securing victory with 26...Ng6 27.Qxf5 Nh6 28.Qg5 Kg7! 29.Qxg3 Rh8 and White is helpless against the discovered check.
27.Rxf5 Qd6?
This gives back most of Black's advantage. Instead he should play 27...Re8, when 28.Rfxe5 Rxe5 29.Qxg4 is met by a crushing reply. (D) (B12) Graif - Ali Maranda 2019 Exercise B12-1: In the position after 27...Re8 28.Rfxe5 Rxe5 29.Qxg4, find a crushing move for Black.
28.Rexe5 Bxe5 29.Qxg4
White recoups his material, and even boxes in Black's king. However, Black's control of the dark squares gives him greater attacking chances.
29...Re8 30.Rg5? (D)
Just when it appears that White is back in the game, he makes a decisive mistake. He is hoping to play Rg8 and trade rooks, but he never gets the chance. The only way to put up further resistance is to play 30.Qh5 instead.
30...Qh6+!
Black's complete domination of the dark squares, coupled with his deep tactical awareness, brings the game to a sudden close.
31.Qh5
White has two other ways to meet this check: 1) If he runs away with 31.Kg1, Black creates deadly threats down the e-file with 31...Bh2+
32.Kf1 Qf6+ 33.Rf5 Qe6, winning material by force.
2) If he blocks with 31.Rh5, Black has the spectacular 31...Bg3! threatening a back-rank mate. After 32.Qxg3 Qxh5+, Black has a comfortably winning position. (31...Bd6 also wins.)
31...Qf6!
Black once again threatens a back-rank mate. Now that White's queen is on h5, White no longer has the resource ...Rg8+. Now if White brings the knight back to block a back-rank check, he runs into a problem along the h-file: 32.Ne2 Bd6 33.Ng1 is met by 33... Ke7! with the threat of ...Rh8.
32.Rf5 (D)
White blocks Black's queen, but does not succeed in eliminating all back-rank threats. After 32.2f5 abcdetgh
32...Bg3!! 0-1
If the queen cannot make it there, the rook can! Mate can no longer be avoided, even at the cost of material. After 44...Ng8 (4O... g8 variation) a b c d e f g, h (B12) Graif - Ali Maranda 2019

43.Rd7 Nb5.
The tempo White gained by attacking the knight on move 42 gives him the same position as after 40.Kf6, but with it being White's turn to move. He now finishes with 44.Kf7 Rg8. (D)
45.Rd8 Rxd8 46.Bg7#. A pleasing smothered mate with king and bishop.
Alekhine-Yates 1923 Exercise B2-1: Find a forced mate after 47.Rh2. (D) After 34...£117 (variation) After 19...fxe6 (variation) Answer: Black mates by inching his queen closer to the white king with checks: 47...Qd3+
48.Kg2 Qe2+ 49.Kg3 Qe3+ 50.Kg2 Qf2+ 51.Kh3 Qf3 mates.

PART C
THE INITIATIVE
he games in this section emphasize the dynamic elements of an attacking game. The dynamic player strives to seize and maintain the initiative, and to deploy his pieces so that they are active and work well together in attack and defense. Critical elements of these games include time, space, and understanding why and when to sacrifice material. Steinitz - von Bardeleben 1895 (Giuoco Piano)
T
LESSON 6
[diagram]
"SAW IT - WENT HOME"
here are two ways to play chess: you can take control yourself, or you can sit back and wait for the play to come to you. The attacking player always strives for the initiative. To gain the initiative, you make a threat that your opponent must meet. To retain the initiative, you continue to operate with threats. This game was played in 1895 by the first official World Champion, Wilhelm Steinitz. Considered the first great master of positional play, Steinitz stressed concepts such as strong squares, weak pawns, and the importance of good technique. In this game, you will see Steinitz build an initiative in the center through a series of trades, then translate it into control of a strong square. Once he establishes that control, he dominates the board. A famous story is told about this game. Steinitz was out of the room when von Bardeleben realized he was lost. He left a note saying "Saw it - went home." What made von Bardeleben give up without formally resigning was something truly amazing, as you will see. The final combination is so stunning that Steinitz is said to have been very upset when he realized he would not get a chance to play it. His only satisfaction was to show it to the spectators after the game.
W. STEINITZ - C. VON BARDELEBEN
Hastings (England) 1895
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bc4 Bc5
The Italian Game, commonly referred to as the Giuoco Piano, is one of the oldest known openings and was played as far back as the early 1500s.

After 6,e5! (4...Bf6 variation)
[diagram]
White plans to extend his pawn chain with 5.d4 in an effort to seize control of the center. Black cannot do anything to stop this. Question: Even 4...Qf6, adding another attacker, is insufficient. Why? Answer: Because the queen is coming out too early and will get chased around. Yes. That's the idea. Black's queen placement can be exploited with a clever pawn sacrifice:
4...Qf6 5.d4 exd4 6.e5!. (D)
Black does not have a good answer to the attack on his queen. Any queen move that does not create an immediate threat simply allows White to gain a tempo and keep the initiative. On the other hand, if Black captures the attacking pawn with 6...Nxe5, White, with 7.Qe2, pins the knight and wins a piece. Neither active defense (7...dxc3 8.Nxe5 Bxf2+ 9.Kd1) nor passive defense (7...d6 8.cxd4) will save the piece. Black has a third option in 6...Qg6, attacking the g2-pawn. Exercise 6-1: After 4...Qf6 5.d4 exd4 6.e5! Qg6, how should White proceed? Black can also try to suppress d2-d4 with 4...Qe7 in the hope of capturing the e4-pawn with check after 5.d4 exd4 6.cxd4. Question: How would you respond to ...Qe7 ? Answer: I would castle. After Steinitz - von Bardeleben 1895 (Giuoco Piano) Very good. After you castle, the threat of d4 is renewed. Black has to be careful because his king and queen are lined up on the same file. After 4...Qe7 5.O-O d6 6.d4, Black must not surrender the center with 6...exd4. Instead, he should hold the center with 6...Bb6.
4...Nf6 (D)
The best move for Black.
5.d4
The popularity of this move has declined in recent years. The quieter alternative 5.d3 is now preferred by leading grandmasters.
5...exd4
Question: What happens if Black chooses not to capture, and plays ...Bb6 ? Answer: White plays d5 attacking the knight, hoping to pick up a pawn. No. That move is bad on two counts. If White tries to win a pawn with 6.d5 Ne7 7.Nxe5, Black does not play 7...Nxe4, as it allows
8.Qg4. Instead Black regains the pawn with 7...d6 8.Nf3 Nxe4. (D)
The second reason d5 does not work is that it opens a diagonal for Black's dark-square bishop and blocks in White's light-square bishop. The variation diagram shows the effect d5 has on the two bishops; Black clearly comes out ahead. After 6...£jxe4 (5...Nb6 variation)

Short-term attacks can have undesirable long-term consequences. Question: Is there a better way to answer ...Bb6 ? Answer: Just play dxe5. Question: Of course! Can you show how White gains a clear advantage after 6.dxe5 Nxe4 ?

6.cxd4 Bb4+ (D)
This active bishop move is the right choice. White has two reasonable moves to block the check: 7.Bd2 and 7.Nc3. Question: After 7.Bd2 Bxd2+ 8.Nbxd2, it is extremely important for Black to find the right move. What is it? Answer: Pawn to d5. Yes. Two unopposed center pawns can advance with devastating effect. Trying to contain them with 8...d6 is too slow, and leaves White in firm control of the center. Black must break up White's connected center pawns immediately. It would have been a mistake for Black to reply to 7.Bd2 with 7...Nxe4. (D) After 7.,.Nxe4 (7.i,d2 variation) After 10...d5 (7.Ad2 variation) Steinitz - von Bardeleben 1895 (Giuoco Piano) White now has a forcing sequence which regains the pawn: 8.Bxb4 Nxb4 9.Bxf7+ Kxf7

Question: Does White have a stronger move than capturing the knight immediately with Qxb4 ? Answer: White can play Ne5+ and get his knight to a strong square. Yes, the intermediate move Ne5+ centralizes the knight and forces Black's king to a worse square. Without this move, Black will be able to castle by hand2 by bringing his rook to e8 and then retreating his king to g8. Look for ways to prevent your opponent's king from reaching safety. 7.Nc3 (D)
This line is now referred to as the Møller Attack, named for the Danish chess theorist Jørgen Møller who, in 1898, published an extensive analysis. White offers to sacrifice his e4-pawn; if Black accepts (7...Nxe4), White castles and gets active play against Black's uncastled king. The line is very complex, but modern theory finds that should both sides play the best moves, the fireworks will fizzle out and leave Black in a better position.
7...d5
Black declines the sacrifice.

After ll.Qb3 (9... xc3 variation) Steinitz - von Bardeleben 1895 (Giuoco Piano) Question: Does Black have time to win White's c3-pawn? Answer: No, it is too dangerous to grab pawns when your king is exposed. Agreed. Going for the pawn is ill-advised. 9...Bxc3 10.bxc3 Nxc3? loses the knight to
11.Qe1+. Of course, Black does not have to take twice on c3. But even a move such as 10...O-O

(D) Exercise 6-3: After 9...Nxc3 10.bxc3 Bxc3 11.Qb3, if Black takes the rook with 11...Bxa1, how does White win? Mobility generally triumphs over material when the defending king is weakened. In any event, von Bardeleben played more cautiously.
9...Be6 10.Bg5 Be7 (D)
This leads to a series of trades that enables White to create pressure along the open e-file and keep Black from castling. 10...Qd7 would have been safer.
11.Bxd5 Bxd5 12.Nxd5 Qxd5 13.Bxe7 Nxe7 (D)
Failing to castle compromises the safety of the king, especially when the e-file is open. Look at how lonely Black's king is. The piece trades have removed his defenders, one by one, and left him exposed in the center. Question: It is clear that White's pieces are more active, but what about his d-pawn - is it strong or weak? Answer: Weak, because it is isolated. Isolated pawns generally are weak because they cannot be protected by another pawn. On the other hand, if the pawn advances to the fifth rank or further, then the space advantage you obtain will give you more room to maneuver in the middlegame and compensate for the inherent weakness of the pawn. Here the isolated pawn is only on the fourth rank, but it is the only center pawn. As such, itstrengthens White's position by controlling key central squares. For the moment, the d4-pawn is strong, but it could become weak as the game continues. White needs to keep this in mind. Question: What is White's next move? Answer: Rook to e1. There is no question about that. After 14.Sei After 14...f6

Steinitz - von Bardeleben 1895 (Giuoco Piano) Black should now centralize his king with 19...Kd6, after which White can pick up a second pawn, giving him a knight and two pawns for the rook. 16...c6? (D)
Black gets distracted by the pressure on c7, and reacts with an overabundance of caution which leads to his downfall. From this point on, the game is beautifully played. Steinitz finds the right plan and executes it with astounding clarity and precision. He focuses on a weak square in Black's position, realizing that posting a knight to this square will dominate the entire board. Question: Which is the weak square? Answer: c5. A good square, but not sufficiently deep in Black's territory to cause trouble. Answer: d6. Much better, but White would have trouble supporting a knight on this square. Answer: e6. This is a lovely square; can the knight get there? You are now playing fantasy chess - pick a square and dream! So long as it is not totally unrealistic, you may have a chance. Question: Is getting the knight to e6 an impossible dream? Answer: No. Play 17.Rcd1 and 18.d5 to create a path for the knight. Steinitz goes one better. He realizes he can play d4-d5 right away. Black cannot take the pawn with the knight or the queen, so he must capture with his pawn. By sacrificing the pawn, White clears the d4 square which his knight will use on its way to e6. This kind of sacrifice is called aclearance sacrifice.
17.d5! cxd5
There is another purpose to sacrificing the pawn. Once Black captures with his pawn, d5 is no longer available as an outpost for his knight. After 18...Nf7

[diagram]
Look for sacrifices that improve your position while simultaneously depriving your opponent of key squares.
18.Nd4

After 26...Nh8 (25...Ng8 variation) abcdefqh The only alternative. Incredibly, all four of White's pieces are threatened, and one of the threatened captures leads to a back-rank mate! White has no time to waste. He has to keep on checking. There are two distinct parts to Steinitz's combination. The key to the first part is to recognize the strength of the pin along the h3-c8 diagonal. Black's queen is pinned to the rook on c8. It can be difficult to see pins when both the pinned piece and the piece executing the pin are the same; in this case, aqueen is pinning a queen. The pin allows White's rook to walk arrogantly along the seventh rank without fear of capture!
23.Rf7+ Kg8 24.Rg7+ Kh8 (D)
Black's king can never move up a rank because White's queen will capture on d7 with check. Capturing the rook is therefore out of the question. Exercise 6-6: If Black's king moves back with 24...Kf8?, how does White respond?

Typically, amating attack involving aqueen would include sweeping moves that emphasize the long-range power of the piece. In this game, however, the queen has moved just one square at a time for six consecutive moves, squeezing Black's king to a slow and painful death.

REVIEW
Themes Control of the center with pawns (4.c3, 5.d4). King in the center (10...d5 11.Ne5+ variation; 13...Nxe7; 14.Re1). Isolated center pawn (discussion after 13...Nxe7). Exchange sacrifice (16...Kf7 17.Qxe7+ variation). Clearance sacrifice (17.d5). Destructive sacrifice (22.Rxe7+). Queen and knight coordination (28.Qh7+ through 35.Qd6+ mate). Advice Short-term attacks can have undesirable long-term consequences. Look for ways to prevent your opponent's king reaching safety. Mobility generally triumphs over material when the defending king is weakened. Do not ignore a problem. It will not go away by itself. Look for sacrifices that improve your position while simultaneously depriving your opponent of key squares. __________ 2 "Castling by hand" means placing a king and rook where they achieve the effect of castling even though castling itself is no longer legal. The rook can choose any square that allows the king to move towards the corner without impeding the rook's development. Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack)
I
LESSON 7
[diagram]
STRIKE WHILE THE KNIGHT IS COLD
n order to become a good chess player, you must above all understand the idea of compensation. You may sacrifice material or structure in return for time and space, with the idea of gaining the initiative. Many intermediate players are reluctant to sacrifice something of value unless they can see an immediate return, but learning to make concessions for dynamic play is part of modern chess. Even though I had not calculated each and every variation to a definite conclusion, Igave up a pawn very early in this game judging that my sacrifice would generate sufficient practical chances. As you will see, it was a matter of seizing the initiative at the proper time. My opponent, Mitchell Goldberg, is a FIDE Master.
S. WEERAMANTRY - M. GOLDBERG
New York, 1991
1.e4 c5 2.Nc3 Nc6 3.f4
White plays the Grand Prix Attack, one of the so-called Anti-Sicilians which refrain from opening the center as in the Open Sicilian (2.Nf3 and 3.d4).3 This system was introduced to me during my early chess-playing days at the Geneva Chess Club. I used it for the first time, with success, against IM Werner Hug in a four-way playoff for the final qualifying spot in the 1967 Under-20 Championship of Switzerland. I was ecstatic when I won the game and the playoffs along with it. This has been one of my favorite openings ever since.
3...e6 4.Nf3 d5 (D)
After 4,..d5 Strong Weaker c d Black immediately contests the center. Instead of 4...d5, his alternatives are 4...Nge7 and
4...a6, after which White's best plan may well be to turn this into an Open Sicilian with 5.d4
cxd4 6.Nxd4. Transpositions are valuable tools for taking your opponent out of his chosen system and compelling him to play yours.
5.Bb5
This is one of the main ideas of the system. White would like to play Bxc6, doubling Black's c-pawns and hoping to use them as targets. The strategy is similar to that used by Black in the Nimzo-Indian Defense, where Black plays ...Bb4 and doubles White's pawns by taking the knight on c3. Black cannot play 5...Bd7 to avoid the doubled pawns, because this would mask the queen's protection of his d5-pawn. Learning to play against doubled pawns is important. Let's look at some typical structures. If you have a three-pawn island that includes a set of doubled pawns, the strongest formation is this: As the single pawn advances, the doubled pawns get weaker because they lose support. This is the weakest formation. Notice how a single opposing pawn cripples all three of Black's pawns. Weakest c d After 7. e2 (5...d4 variation) Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) I have enjoyed tremendous success over the years playing against this particular pawn formation in the Grand Prix. When your opponent has virtually no pawn play, it is easy to predict his plans and keep the game under control. A fluid pawn structure complicates the game and makes long-term planning more difficult. Fixed structures are easier to play against.
5...Nf6 (D)
Other moves are inferior. 5...a6 is a wasted move that simply encourages White to do what he planned in the first place. If 5...d4 or 5...dxe4, White still gets to capture on c6, weakening Black's pawn structure. As we have already examined the pawn-structure weaknesses resulting from the doubled c- pawns, let us look at the position that would have arisen after 5...d4 6.Bxc6+ bxc6 7.Ne2. (D) After 6.d3
[diagram]
After 9.&xdl (6...dxe4 variation)
[diagram]
If White is given the opportunity, he will cripple Black's c- and d-pawns with 8.d3 and further strengthen his hold over the c4 square with 9.b3. To avoid this, Black could try 7...d3. However, after 8.cxd3 Qxd3 9.Qa4 Qb5 10.Nc3, White has excellent play against Black's weakened pawn structure.
6.d3 (D)
The common move order, which I have frequently employed, is 6.Bxc6 bxc6 7.e5. However, after 7...Nd7 8.d3 Ba6, I have had trouble with the ...c5-c4 pawn thrust. The move 6.d3 is an attempt on my part to delay Black's counterplay. After all, the c8-bishop cannot go to a6 until Black's b-pawn has moved. The only drawback to 6.d3 would be if Black decided to trade queens with 6...dxe4, a move played against me in 1992 by National Master Ralph Zimmer at a tournament in Somerset, New Jersey. Although I lost that game, Iwas quite happy with my position after 7.Bxc6+ bxc6
8.dxe4 Qxd1+ 9.Kxd1 (D) and would not mind tackling this sequence again.
Black's structural weaknesses and lack of space in the center present him with some difficulties. White should be able to exploit this despite the fact that his king is uncastled. If the above variation holds up to closer scrutiny, my move order with 6.d3 would represent an improvement. In this game, Goldberg opts not to trade queens and plays a routine developing move.
6...Be7 7.O-O (D)
Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Should Black now castle, White will continue with 8.Bxc6 bxc6 9.e5. By delaying the capture on c6, White makes the ...Ba6 and ...c5-c4 idea less effective. He would have time to play b2-b3 and better control c4.
7...Bd7
Black seeks to avoid the doubled pawns. While this position is assessed as equal, Iwas now in unfamiliar territory. This was the first time I had encountered this exact sequence. When you find yourself in a new position, you must remember to take your time and consider how the position differs from what you already know. Do not rush your reply.
8.e5 Ng8? (D)
Black should have tried 8...Ng4. Retreating the knight to the first rank should be a last resort because it will take time to get it back into the game. By inducing Black's knight to go back to its starting square, White gained a lead in development. Indeed, White has gained two tempi. But remember, an advantage in development/mobility is temporary. Given sufficient time, Black will recover. The best way to exploit the advantage in development is to immediately force open the position. Look for a central pawn advance - even a sacrifice - that changes the pawn structure. This game reminded me of one played by one of my students at the 1987 World Youth Championships in Puerto Rico. White tried to relocate the c3-knight to the kingside by moving back to d1. The moment the knight hit d1, Black advanced a center pawn. Suddenly White's After 9.f5 After 9...exf5
[diagram]
entire position disintegrated, just in the space of that one move. You will not always have a center pawn to advance, but that is what you should look for. Question: So, what's the best move? Answer: White should play 9.d4. Then, if9...cxd4, 10.Nxd4 opens the center. Yes, it does trade pawns, but does not radically change the pawn structure. Answer: Play g4 and prepare f5. You are getting close, but this does not force open the position. If the other player has already castled on the kingside, you might consider this plan. For now, it is too slow. A loss in time must be exploited immediately. Answer: Play f5 right away.
9.f5 (D)
Good timing! If White does not play f4-f5 immediately, Black will stop it with 9...Nh6 and be ready to get his king out of the center. The f4-pawn was hindering the mobility of White's c1-bishop and fl-rook. Get rid of the f4- pawn and see how these two pieces come alive! New lines of attack will open up immediately.
9...exf5 (D)
10.Nxd5 Nxe5 11.Nxe5 Bxb5
Question: After 11...Bxb5, what should White play? After 12.c4
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Answer: Play 12.c4, protecting the knight and attacking the b5-bishop.
12.c4 (D)
White is still down a pawn but has two strong centralized knights and pressure on the f5- pawn. He is castled and has better development. This is the position I had envisioned when I decided to play 9.f5. I had not analyzed beyond this point because I had judged that I had ample compensation for the pawn. When analyzing long variations, understanding where to pause your analysis is important. Save time on your clock and think on your opponent's time. Now, Black must move the bishop before he can free his position. Attempting to complete his development with 12...Nf6 leads to disaster. Exercise 7-1: Find White's best reply against 12...Nf6. If instead Black plays 12...Bc6, White gains a clear advantage with 13.Rxf5 Bxd5 14.cxd5 Nf6 15.Qa4+ forcing Black to move his king and depriving him of the right to castle. So the bishop goes back to d7.
12...Bd7 13.Bf4 Be6
Question: This threatens ...Bxd5. How does White proceed? Answer: Qa4+. Good. Look for forcing moves. Black's knight and rook are never going to get out.
14.Qa4+ Kf8 (D)
After 14...Qf8
[diagram]
After 15.Nc7
[diagram]
Black has no choice but to move the king. 14...Bd7, blocking the check, loses. Exercise 7-2: If 14...Bd7, how does White win material? Black's best hope is to continue with ...g7-g6 in order to sneak his king to safety on g7. Question: How does White keep his attack going? Answer: 15.Nc7. If 15...Qxc7, 16.Ng6+ wins the queen. A pretty move. You definitely have a good tactical sense. White tempts Black's queen to capture on c7 in order to set up a discovered attack. This is referred to as a magnet combination, or combination of attraction.
15.Nc7 (D)
This is a difficult position for Black to defend. Sometimes, it may be wise to give up a little material to secure active counterplay. This is one of those positions. Black's best defense would be to abandon the exchange and create much needed space for his king with 15...g5 16.Bg3 f4.
(D)
After 16...Rd8
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) White now has two choices. He can take the rook with Nxa8, or the bishop with Nxe6+. Question: Which plan would you choose? Answer: Nxa8 wins material. Your move does win the exchange, but it gives Black good compensation. After 17.Nxa8 fxg3
18.Qxa7 Bd6, Black's bishop pair becomes very active. White's knight is stuck in the corner and
his queen is out of play. Answer: Nxe6+. Yes. Taking the bishop eliminates a key defender and weakens Black's king.
15...Qd4+
Black does not find the above defense.
16.Kh1 Rd8 (D)
Question: Find the best way to continue the attack. Answer: Qd1, then move over to h5, setting up Ng6+. Redeploying pieces is often an important concept. Here it is not warranted. Look at Black's pieces: most of his army is on the kingside. Why move the queen over to the side of the board where Black's pieces are concentrated? Attack where the enemy is weak. Answer: Qb3 attacks the b7-pawn. After 18.aael After 19...Nc8
[diagram]
Excellent. This is even stronger than what I played. The point, however, is not the pawn; it is that White now threatens Nb5 trapping Black's queen. Answer: Qxa7. You would go pawn hunting in this position? This is also a strong move, and is indeed what I played.
17.Qxa7
We have already observed that Black's pieces are huddled on the kingside. The queenside pawns are left defenseless. Who says you always need a combination to win? Just queen the a- pawn!
17...Bf6 18.Rae1 (D)
This brings the last piece into the game. Unlike Black, White has achieved full development. He is getting ready to capture the b-pawn and Black cannot protect it. Exercise 7-3: If Black responds with 18...Bxe5 19.Bxe5 Qxd3, what does White do?
18...Ne7 19.Qxb7 Bc8 (D)
Question: Where should White's queen go? Answer: b6. An excellent square. Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack)
20.Qb6
The queen is perfectly placed on b6, where it applies pressure on the f6-bishop and prevents the freeing move ...g7-g6. Black cannot play 20... Rd6, as 21.Ng6+ sets up a discovered attack on the rook. At this point, the game is essentially over. Black offers no resistance.

ANSWERS
Exercise 7-1: Find White's best reply against 12...Nf6. (D) Answer: White wins a piece with 13.Nxe7 Qxe7 14.cxb5 Qxe5 15.Re1 Ne4 16.dxe4. No better is
13...Qd4+ 14.Kh1 Qxe5 15.Nxf5, threatening both Re1 and cxb5.
Exercise 7-2: If 14...Bd7 (D), how does White win material? Answer: White wins material with 15.Nxd7 Qxd7 16.Nc7+ Kd8 17.Qxd7+ Kxd7 18.Nxa8. Now Black must play 18...Bd6 to gain back a knight for the lost rook, but White wins two pawns for it: 19.Bxd6 Kxd6 20.Rxf5 Nf6 (20...b6 21.Rd5+) 21.Nc7 Kxc7 22.Rxc5+. Exercise 7-3: If Black responds with 18...Bxe5 19.Bxe5 Qxd3 (D), what does White do? After 19...Qxd3 (18...jLxe5 variation) Weeramantry - Goldberg 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Answer: White wins with a series of checks: 20.Qxc5+ Ne7 21.Bxg7+ Kxg7 22.Nxe6+ leading to mate or to the win of the queen.
REVIEW
Themes Weaknesses of doubled pawn formations (5...d4 6.Bxc6+ variation). Surrendering the right to castle to secure an advantage in structure and space (9.Kxd1 variation). Incurring a loss of time by retreating a knight to the first rank (discussion after 8...Ng8). Opening the position to exploit an advantage in mobility (9.f5). Supporting the center (12.c4). Attacking on the weak side (17.Qxa7). Advice Fixed structures are easier to play against. When you find yourself in a new position, you must remember to take your time and consider how the position differs from what you already know. Do not rush your reply. A loss in time must be exploited immediately. When analyzing long variations, understanding where to pause your analysis is important. Save time on your clock and think on your opponent's time. Attack where the enemy is weak. __________

LESSON 8
[diagram]
A MISSED OPPORTUNITY
his is one of the most memorable games I ever played. At one point I came up with a theoretical novelty in a seldom played variation of the Max Lange Attack. This game has been published all over the world. My opponent was the reigning champion of Germany, IM Hans-Joachim Hecht, who was awarded the GM title three years later. As a young untested player, I was terrified to face him. Even so, Iwas able to play aggressively and create good winning chances.
S. WEERAMANTRY - H. HECHT
Bagneux Open (France) 1970
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.d4 exd4 4.Bc4
This game begins as a Scotch Gambit. White refrains from recapturing the pawn in order to develop more quickly.
4...Nf6 (D)
Black adopts the Two Knights' Defense. A good alternative is4...Bc5. If White then plays 5.c3, Black can reply 5...Nf6. After 5...Sf6 (4... ,c5 variation) 8 7 6 5 4 3 2 1 abode fgh After 5."Nc5
[diagram]
After 8*£jc3 (5..*Nxe4 variation)
[diagram]
This offers to transpose into the main line of the Giuoco Piano after 6.cxd4 Bb4+.4 White can also continue with 6.e5 d5 7.Bb5 Ne4 8.cxd4 Bb4+. The position is even.5
5.O-O Bc5 (D)
Black invites the Max Lange Attack. Instead, Black could have played 5...Nxe4, the main line of the Two Knights' Defense. White can pin this knight with 6.Re1, but Black defends with 6...d5. Black's two extra pawns appear to give him the advantage. However, White now has a powerful sequence with a temporary piece sacrifice: 7.Bxd5! Qxd5 8.Nc3. (D) After 6.e5 After 7,*,dxc4 Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) An unusual sight: Black's knight and pawn are both pinned. Now, after 8...Qa5 9.Nxe4 Be6
10.Bd2, White will regain his sacrificed material with correct play.
6.e5 (D)
The Max Lange Attack. White has accepted the challenge. Question: What should Black do now? Answer: ...Ne4. This will run into a very uncomfortable pin after 7.Qe2 d5 8.exd6 f5 9.Bg5 Qxd6 10.Nbd2. Black's king is unable to get out of the center. Answer: ...Ng4. This move enjoyed a bad reputation for many years, but has now been rehabilitated. It is worth closer study, but that will be for another time. Answer: ...d5.
6...d5
The most reliable move. Establishing the d-pawn in the center neutralizes the attacking potential of White's c4-bishop.
7.exf6 dxc4 (D)
Both sides have just captured a piece: White has taken the f6-knight, Black the bishop on c4. After 9...mf6 (variation)
[diagram]
Question: This is trade for equal value. Has either side gained an advantage in position? Answer: Yes. Black. The c4- and d4-pawns are very strong. True, they work very well together. The d4-pawn is well protected and the c4-pawn is easily defended with ...Be6. Black's plan would be to castle queenside and eventually create a passed pawn with ...d3. Question: And, what about White's f6-pawn? Answer: It can capture on g7 and attack the rook. Yes, but Black will reply with ...Rg8 and use the g-file to attack White's king. If White keeps this pawn on f6, it will prevent Black from blocking a check along the e-file by moving a piece to e7.
8.Re1+ Be6 9.Ng5
White piles the pressure on the pinned piece. It appears that Black can defend the bishop for a second time with 9...Qxf6 (D) and eliminate White's dangerous f6-pawn at the same time. Question: Is this a good idea for Black? Answer: No. White can keep increasing the pressure on e6 with Qg4. Be careful - that exposes the queen. Black would castle kingside, breaking the pin on the e6- bishop, and if White continued with 11.Nxe6, Black would recapture with the f-pawn and open up a powerful attack along the f-file. Answer: No. After 9...Qxf6, Black loses a piece to 10.Nxe6 fxe6 11.Qh5+, winning the c5- bishop by force. Excellent. Always examine forcing moves. It will help avoid blunders.
9...Qd5 10.Nc3 (D)
After 10-Nc3 8 6 5 3 2
[diagram]
After 12.g4 (ll. ce4 variation)
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) White brings his c3-knight into action with tempo. Of course, Black cannot capture this knight as it would cost him his queen. Black must now move his queen and keep his e6-bishop protected. The only squares that allow this are d7, d6, and f5. If Black plays 10...Qd6 or 10...Qd7, White replies with 11.Nce4, threatening the c5-bishop. Once the bishop moves, White continues with a strong attack featuring moves like fxg7, Nxe6, and Qh5+.
10...Qf5 11.g4 (D)
An obscure move that had only been played a handful of times before this game; the more established line is11.Nce4 O-O-O 12.g4. (D) In this position, Black has three choices. The first, 12...Qxg4+, loses material. Exercise 8-1: After 11.Nce4 O-O-O 12.g4 Qxg4+, how does White win material? Question: What else can the queen do? Answer: Move to d5. No, that also loses the exchange: 13.fxg7 Rhg8 14.Nf6 with a double attack on the queen and rook. Answer: ...Qe5. This is the best square. You do not have to fear a discovered attack because the e4-knight cannot cause immediate damage. After 13.fxg7 Rhg8 14.Nxe6 fxe6, White should protect his g7- After 15.i,h6 (11.&ce4 variation) After ll."@xf6
[diagram]
After 12,"$d8
[diagram]
pawn with 15.Bh6. (D) There is a dynamic balance to this position. Each side has plenty to play for.
11...Qxf6 (D)
Now Black's queen can safely capture the pawn on f6. Instead of taking the pawn, the preferred move is 11...Qg6 as in Black-burne - Teichmann, Nuremberg 1896. I had come across that game in Modern Chess Openings 10th edition, which was the only book on opening theory I owned at that time.
12.Nd5 Qd8 (D)
After 14...Bh4? (variation) Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) It is amusing to note that Black's queen made a loop going from d8 to d5 to f5 to f6 and back to d8.
13.Rxe6+
This enterprising exchange sacrifice offers ample compensation.
13...fxe6 14.Nxe6 (D)
While Black's rooks are standing idly in the corners, White's knights have taken up menacing positions in the center. The e6-knight, in particular, is attacking Black's queen. The knights are positioned in such a way that should the queen capture one of them, the other knight will take on c7 forking Black's king and queen. Question: Where can the queen go? Answer: ...Qh4. That would be a disaster for Black. Iactually reached this position once and my opponent resigned two moves later. (D) Question: What made him resign? Answer: Play the bishop to g5. That chases the queen to h3, where it can be trapped by moving a knight to f4. Precisely. It is amazing that the queen cannot go anywhere safe, even though the entire third rank is open. After 15>Nh6 Question: Where else can the queen go, besides h4? Answer: ...Qd6. This only helps White to develop more quickly after Bf4. Exercise 8-2: After 14...Qd6 15.Bf4 Qd7, how does White continue the attack? As his queen cannot capture either knight, Black has nothing better than to retreat to d7 and concede a valuable tempo.
14...Qd7
White now plays a surprising move.
15.Bh6 (D)
This move was an over-the-board inspiration and not the result of home preparation. Remarkably, all three of White's minor pieces are hanging, yet none can be captured without Black's losing his queen to a knight fork. Although this move had never been played before, at least according to ChessBase, it turns out 15.Bh6 had been suggested by Staunton in his analysis of Steinitz - Meitner, Vienna 1860. Such chess information was not readily available to the average player back in 1970. Today, chess engines recommend 15.Ndxc7+ Kf7 16.Ng5+ Kg8 17.Nxa8 with a winning advantage.
15...Bd6
Black decides to abandon the immediate point of attack (g7) and chooses to defend c7 instead. There is a common military tactic in which an army is sometimes forced to abandon an outer area in order to fall back and regroup around a more defensible position.
16.Nxg7+
Question: How would Black have replied if White had played Bxg7 instead? Answer: ...Qxe6. Yes. Now that c7 is defended, the knight can be captured. 17.Nf6+ can be met with 17...Kf7, and Black gets two pieces for the rook.
16...Kd8 17.Nf6 (D)
After 18.Qf3 Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) White's knights are still in attacking positions, but they are not as well coordinated as earlier.
17...Qe7
Black attacks the f6-knight, but it is better to attack it from f7. Then, if 18.Qf3, White's f6- knight will be pinned to his queen and his overall mobility hindered.
18.Qf3 (D)
White gets his queen into action and protects his f6-knight. The queen's presence on the long light-square diagonal prevents Black from playing ...Ne5 because his b7-pawn will be left unprotected. Also, should Black pin White's f6-knight with 18...Rf8, White will secure his knight with 19.g5 and open the h3-c8 diagonal with aview to controlling the light squares. Question: Black does not have any good moves for his knight or his h8-rook. So, what can he move? Answer: Move the queen to e5, threatening ...Qxh2+ forking the king and bishop. (D) After 18...ffie5 (variation) abode f g h After 18".Nc8
[diagram]
Question: White has an unusual move to stop that threat. What is it? Answer: Bf4. Yes. The bishop cannot be taken because Ne6+ would fork Black's king and queen. Question: What if he takes the f6-knight instead? Answer: Offer the bishop again with Bg5. Exactly. The pin and fork sequence again wins Black's queen. Unfortunately, Black does not have to capture the bishop or the knight. He can retreat his queen to e7 and White will have nothing better than to repeat the position with Bh6. Therefore, Bf4 is good enough for a draw by repetition, but White can play for more. Exercise 8-3: After 18...Qe5, what should White play? After thinking for about 40 minutes, Hecht came up with a simple but effective plan that did not involve moving his queen, rook, or knight.
18...Kc8 (D)
Black quietly walks his king away from the action. His intention is to play ...a7-a6 at the first opportunity and shelter his king on a7. Once there, his rooks will be connected and his extra material will prevail.
19.Qf5+ Kb8 20.Nd7+

After 23.>.Ne7 (2L?bc5+ variation)
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) Visions of smothered mates were dancing around in my head. Sadly, Black's queen is ideally placed to prevent it.
20...Kc8 (D)
21.Nb6+
I played the standard pattern for a smothered mate and did not consider the option of
21.Nc5+. I failed to realize the importance of keeping pressure on b7 so that Black would not
have the defensive resource that he gets later, on move 23. After 21.Nc5+ Kb8 22.Bg5 Qxg7
23.Bf6, Black will have to retreat his queen to g8. The counterattack with 23...Ne7 does not
work here. (D) White wins with the intermediate move 24.Qe4, threatening mate on b7 while still attacking Black's queen. Black cannot defend both threats.
21...Kb8 22.Bg5
22.Nd7+ would have given White the chance to get back into the 21.Nc5+ variation we just
discussed.
22...Qxg7 23.Bf6 (D)
23...Qg8?
Retreating the queen keeps the threat of smothered mate under control. However, Black misses the powerful defensive resource 23...Ne7. The counterattack works here because, without a knight on c5, White cannot play Qe4 to generate a mate threat on b7.
24.Nd7+ Kc8 (D)
25.Bxh8?
A horrendous move. Why trade an active piece for one that absolutely has no influence on the position? Indeed, White misses his final chance to secure a winning position. The correct move is 25.Re1. White is threatening to mate with Nf8+ followed by Re8. Black must create an escape square for his king by moving either his a- or b-pawn. Exercise 8-4: After 25.Re1 a6, what is White's best move? Black can put up more resistance with 25...b6, but White still retains the advantage after
26.Nxb6+ Kb7 27.Nxa8 (D)
After 27.&Bxa8 (25.Sei variation) After 26...-Se? Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack)
27...a6 28.Nxc7 Bxc7 29.Re6. White delays capturing on h8 in order to set up more threats
in the center. White is winning.
25...Qxh8 26.Re1 Ne7 (D)
Black attacks White's queen, but White has one final trick up his sleeve.
27.Nf8+
Black cannot capture the queen as he would blunder into mate with 28.Re8#. Hecht, who had been looking intently at the board, raised his head and smiled. Realizing he was not about to fall for this, I extended my hand to offer a draw. After 27...Kb8, the game will end in a perpetual check. We shook hands and thus ended a game I would remember for the rest of my life. After 12...9xg4+ (ll.ftce4 variation)

ANSWERS
Exercise 8-1: After 11.Nce4 O-O-O 12.g4 Qxg4+ (D), how does White win material? Answer: White plays 13.Qxg4 Bxg4 14.Nxf7 gxf6 15.Nxh8, winning the exchange. Although at first glance 14.Nxc5 looks more appealing, that move creates a complex position where Black's pawns provide ample compensation for the piece. Exercise 8-2: After 14...Qd6 15.Bf4 Qd7 (D), how does White continue the attack? Answer: White wins material with a series of forcing moves: 16.Ndxc7+ Kf7 17.Nxc5 Qe7 18.Qf3 Kg8 19.Nxa8 Qxc5 20.Re1. Exercise 8-3: After 18...Qe5, what should White play? (D) After 18...iBe5 (variation) After 25",a6 (25,Rel variation)
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Hecht 1970 (Max Lange Attack) Answer: White can play 19.Qh3 protecting h2. Now 19...Qxf6 loses to 20.Bg5 Qxg5 21.Ne6+ winning the queen. Of course, 19...Qxf6 is not forced, but White will still secure the advantage with 20.g5. Exercise 8-4: After 25.Re1 a6 (D), what is White's best move? Answer: White's best move is26.Bxh8 Qxh8 27.Nf6+ Kb8 28.Re8+ Qxe8 29.Nxe8. White's active pieces and kingside pawn majority give him a clear advantage.
REVIEW
Themes Sacrifice of the exchange for positional compensation (introduction). Opening move transpositions and choices - Scotch Opening, Scotch Gambit, Giuoco Piano, Max Lange Attack (moves 1-5). Fighting for the center (6...d5). Attack and counterattack: piece values and the initiative (6...d5). "Monster" black pawns on c4 and d4 (7...dxc4). Doubled pawns can be strong (10.Nc3 comment). The theoretical line 11.Nce4 in the Max Lange Attack. Pseudo- (or sham) versus real sacrifices (13.Rxe6+). Strong centralized knights (14.Nxe6). The more practical approach (15.Ndxc7+ variation). Pulling the trigger too soon (25.Bxh8). Maintaining the threat versus choosing the execution (game conclusion). Advice Always examine forcing moves. It will help avoid blunders. As Black, you should always take the Scotch Gambit pawn, but you must know all the traps and gambits. In many openings, the move ...d5 can be a demanding answer for Black in the fight for the center. When you counterattack, you generally want the threat to be against a piece of greater value than the one that was attacked, else the first player to capture gets the initiative. Be alert to the common motif of a queen check on one side of the board to pick up a piece on the other side. To evaluate pawn structure, not only must you look at individual pawns and squares, but also at the pawn structure as a whole. To decide when to "pull the trigger," first examine lines thoroughly, then make a decision based on your experience and intuition. __________
4 See Lesson 6, Steinitz-von Bardeleben.
5 See Illustrative Game B10, Weeramantry-Kumar.

25.Nb5 (D)
White is pinning his hopes on a queenside breakthrough. Anticipating this shift of White's forces, Black prepares an invasion on the opposite wing.

The point of this sacrifice is to begin clearing the h4-d8 diagonal.

This exchange sacrifice removes the threat on c7.

After 31.Bxf3 (C4) Tal - Smyslov 1959 In capturing the rook, White's queen has to abandon the kingside. White's pieces can no longer rush over to help his king.

T
PART D
COORDINATION AND CONTROL
he games in this final section illustrate the establishment of positional advantages that allow a player to control the course of the game, and the placement and coordination of pieces to support attack and defense. You will see the use of many of the positional and dynamic elements embodied in earlier lessons. Critical elements of these games include material imbalances, superior pawn structure, controlling lines of attack, and restricting the opponent's activity. After 3...Qf6
[diagram]
P
LESSON 9
[diagram]
PLAYING WITH A FULL DECK
aul Morphy is regarded as one of the greatest chess players in history. This memorable game was played at the American Chess Congress in 1857 at the beginning of his illustrious career. His opponent was Louis Paulsen, who was already established as a world-class player. In the game, Morphy seizes the opportunity to cripple Paulsen's pieces by taking absolute control of the center and preventing his opponent from coordinating his pieces effectively. This control ultimately translates into a decisive kingside attack.
L. PAULSEN - P. MORPHY
New York 1857
1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Nc3 Nf6 (D)
This is the Four Knights Game. There are three good moves for White in this position. However, 4.Bc4 is not one of them. Even though c4 is a natural developing square for the bishop, especially in double king-pawn openings, it does not work here. Question: Why isBc4 a bad move? Answer: Black's knight can take the e4-pawn. If White accepts the sacrifice, Black can regain the piece with ...d5. After 6-.,Bxd5 (4jg,c4 variation) After 6...d5 (4,jLc4 variation)
[diagram]
Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) That is correct. The key move is 4...Nxe4, a move that gives Black better control of the center in all variations. This is called the center fork trick. For example, after 4.Bc4 Nxe4 5.Nxe4 d5 6.Bxd5 Qxd5 (D), material is even, and Black has an established center pawn, while White does not. The presence of the center pawn is very important; it controls key squares and gives Black a space advantage. Black also has better mobility as both bishops have open diagonals and the queen is centralized. Alternatively, White could play 5.Bxf7+, electing to expose Black's king by sacrificing his bishop before capturing on e4. However, after 5... Kxf7 6.Nxe4 d5 (D), Black's powerful center more than compensates for losing his right to castle. Remember the center fork trick. While it does not win material, it does give you a powerful position. White chooses one of the three reliable moves. The others are 4.d4 and 4.g3.
4.Bb5 (D)
After 6.£jxe5 (5Jbcc6 variation)
[diagram]
Black's most popular reply here is 4...Bb4, which sets up a symmetrical position. If I fold the board in the center, all the opposing pieces will overlap, one on one. Symmetrical positions, if they stay that way, tend to lead to quiet games because they contain balanced pawn structures. On the other hand, assymetrical structures allow more pawn contact and thus create more dynamic play. An attempt by Black to create more active play is 4...Nd4, the Rubinstein Variation. I do not recommend this move to intermediate-level players because it moves the same piece twice in the opening. It is better to concentrate on normal piece development and to observe basic opening principles. Morphy chose a different move entirely.
4...Bc5
Question: How can White now win a pawn? Answer: 5.Bxc6 dxc6 6.Nxe5. (D) Question: Should White go for the pawn? Answer: No, because Black then has 6...Qd4, threatening both the knight and mate on f2. True, but after 6...Qd4, White can protect against both threats with 7.Nd3 (D). White would be in an awkward position because the knight on d3 blocks his d-pawn, but he would be holding on to his extra pawn. After 7.Nd3 (5JLxc6 variation) Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) Exercise 9-1: After 5.Bxc6 dxc6 6.Nxe5 Qd4 7.Nd3, if Black now tries to regain the pawn with 7...Nxe4, what will happen? Instead of 6...Qd4, Black can gain the advantage by first sacrificing the bishop: 6...Bxf2+
7.Kxf2 Qd4+ 8.Ke1 Qxe5. Black wins back the piece and also stops White from castling.
5.O-O
The better move for White appears to be the immediate 5.Nxe5.
5...O-O (D)
6.Nxe5
Question: Why is White taking a pawn that is defended? Answer: White now has the same center fork trick that we saw earlier for Black. You recognized it! Paulsen is trying the center fork trick on Morphy. It is important to recognize a pattern even when colors are reversed. If Black plays 6...Nxe5, White replies 7.d4 Bd6 8.f4!, getting his piece back with a strong center. Morphy has other ideas. Rather than take the time to capture the knight, he plays a move that furthers his development.

After 8.Ac4 a b ed e t g h This plays into Black's hands. White should give the pawn back immediately with 7.Nf3 Nxe4. After 8.Nxe4 Rxe4 9.c3, White retains a slight advantage.
7...dxc6
This is the correct way to recapture. Question: Why is it better to take with the d-pawn? Answer: To open the d-file. You get more mobility and faster development. Question: True, but is there something else that should be noted about this move? Answer: The pawn is not capturing towards the center. Good. There is a general principle which states that pawns should capture towards the center. This helps strengthen the central pawn structure. However, there is an important lesson here: do not blindly follow general principles. There are often exceptions. Morphy recognizes that, in this position, it is more important to open up lines as quickly as possible.
8.Bc4 (D)
8.Be2 immediately would have been a better choice.
8...b5
8...Ng4 is an interesting alternative first suggested by the British chess historian Philip
Sergeant in 1916. The idea is to target f2 and h2 at the same time. The immediate threat is to Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) capture twice on f2, then pick up the c4-bishop with...Qd4+. White should counter with 9.g3, leading to a complex position. Exercise 9-2: If instead 8...Nxe4, find the best continuation for White.
9.Be2
White chooses e2 for his bishop, rather than b3, in order to add more defense to the kingside. Notice how Black's bishops are pointing toward White's king, the knight is eyeballing g4, the rook is on the e-file, and the queen is only one move away from joining the other attackers. Do you see that? White needs more resources to counter Black's pressure.
9...Nxe4
Now that White has strengthened his control of g4, Black takes back his pawn. Otherwise, White will simply protect it with d3.
10.Nxe4 Rxe4 (D)
An unusual picture. Although ten moves have been completed, it looks as though White has not made any moves. All of his pieces are still on the first two ranks. Question: How should White continue his development? Answer: Pawn to d3. A solid developing move. The simplest solutions are often the best.

Question: White sets up a skewer. Where should Black move his rook? Answer: ...Re6, to protect the c6-pawn.
11...Re6
It is ironic that the bishop's attack forces Black's rook to a good square. Usually you do not get the chance to carry out a rook lift this early in the game, because rooks have trouble coming out safely with so many minor pieces still on the board. At the appropriate time, Black can swing this rook over to the g- or h-files to set up a direct attack on the king.
12.c3?! (D)
Question: Why does White play his pawn to c3? Answer: So he can play d4 next. Yes, this is White's plan. If he succeeds in extending his pawn chain to d4, he will take over the center. However, this move is too ambitious. White's priority should be to activate his c1- bishop with d3. In this position, the attempt to build the longer pawn chain is seriously flawed. Question: How does Black take advantage of White's inaccurate move? Answer: ...Qd3 stops the d-pawn from advancing. Excellent. We have already spoken about not spearheading an attack with the queen. Here, the queen's function is not one of attack; it is one of restriction. You may sometimes hear the After 12,"$d3 After 14.14?
[diagram]
Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) word prophylactic used to refer to a move like this. It is a move that is designed to prevent your opponent's intended move.
12...Qd3 (D)
This move is not a check. It is not a capture. It is not even attacking an unprotected piece, yet it is such a powerful move! It blocks the d-pawn, buries the c1-bishop, and paralyzes the queenside. Black's queen cannot easily be dislodged from d3, because Black is controlling the central light squares.
13.b4
Rather than playing on the queenside, White could have tried 13.Re1 seeking to free his position by trading rooks.
13....Bb6 14.a4? (D)
Continuing his queenside expansion, White threatens to trap Black's bishop with 15.a5. Again, it would be better to offer a trade of rooks with 14.Re1. White misses his last chance to challenge the center.
14...bxa4 15.Qxa4 (D)
After 15.Bxa4 After 16,Sa2? When you drive a wedge down the middle of the enemy position, his right and left flanks cannot communicate easily as the space available to shift pieces from one side of the board to the other is very narrow. Black's queen on d3 dominates this position. While White continues to expand, it is not happening in the center. Question: How should Black continue? Answer: 15...Rg6, pinning the g-pawn and threatening to capture the bishop. Not a bad move, but Morphy did not do that. The threat is too simple. There is a lesson you should learn from Morphy's games: a great attacker never attacks without getting all of his pieces out first.
15...Bd7
A simple developing move that gets the bishop out and frees the rook. However, it would have been better to play ...Bb7 to keep White's queen out of a6.
16.Ra2? (D)
What a strange square for the rook! White likely intends to challenge Black's queen with
17.Qc2. If so, he is taking far too long. He has just missed a great chance to drive Black's queen
out of d3 immediately with 16.Qa6. Question: How should Black continue? Answer: Double the rooks. After 17,Qa6 abcdefgn Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) Now you are playing like Morphy!
16...Rae8
I cannot stress this enough: attacks are much more successful with full development. Black's rooks are connected while White's are not. White is wishing that his a-rook had never left the back rank. Question: What is Black's threat? Answer: Sacrifice the queen with ...Qxf1+, and then ...Re1#. Excellent. This is aforcing sequence, and therefore clearer than playing ...Re1 first. Creating an escape square does not save White: h3 does not prevent ...Qxf1+, while g3 leaves the f3- bishop hanging.
17.Qa6 (D)
This is referred to as an X-ray defense. Like an X-ray passing through a body, its line of defense passes through an enemy piece to protect a square further along that line. Thus, White's queen on a6 is defending the f1-rook despite the presence of Black's queen on d3. Now 17...Qxf1+ 18.Qxf1 Re1 19.Bb2 is just a fancy trade. Question: Qd1 would also defend against mate. Why did White choose Qa6 instead? Answer: Because he wants to trade off queens. Yes. Qd1 does not break the bind. Unfortunately, Qa6 has come too late to be effective. This is the critical position. White is on the verge of driving Black's pieces back, but Morphy finds a brilliant move to keep his attack alive.
17...Qxf3!! (D)
After 19.&hl
[diagram]
A stunning and unexpected sacrifice! Black shatters White's castled position in one tremendous blow. When playing a destructive sacrifice, it is important to ensure there are sufficient resources remaining after the sacrifice to complete the attack.
18.gxf3 Rg6+ 19.Kh1 (D)
The concluding sequence of this combination contains a valuable lesson. When executing a mating attack, whenever possible, limit the movement of the defending king. Even though Morphy does miss a quicker win on two separate occasions, he does not let White's king slither out of the mating net. Question: How does Black continue the attack? Answer: Open the long diagonal with...c5. No, that is too slow. Remember, once you sacrifice material, it is important to pursue the attack actively and aggressively. You do not always have to capture or deliver check, but you must make moves that threaten. In order to justify a queen sacrifice, the threat must be powerful. It should be equal to or greater than the value of the sacrificed piece. Question: How can Black create a serious threat? Answer: ...Bh3. Question: That's the move. What is the threat? After ZO.Edl
[diagram]
Paulsen - Morphy 1857 (Four Knights Game) Answer: 20...Bg2+ 21.Kg1 Bxf3#. Good. That is the real threat - Black is not interested in capturing the f1-rook. He did not sacrifice his queen to win back an exchange.
19...Bh3 (D)
Attempts by White to avert mate by guarding critical squares or returning material will not work. Exercise 9-3: If White protects the g2 square with 20.Rg1, Black has two different ways to checkmate. Can you show them? Since the g2 square cannot be defended adequately, White could instead try removing a key attacker. He can afford to sacrifice the queen for the rook on g6 because he is ahead in material. Unfortunately, this attempt at defense also fails: 20.Qd3 would be met by 20...f5 re-establishing the original threat. Now 21.Qc4+ is met by 21...Kf8, after which White cannot defend against all threats. The only way for White to avert mate is to move the f1-rook and create an escape square for his king.
20.Rd1 (D)
Black now initiates a forcing sequence.

23.Kg1
Now that the f3-pawn is no longer there, Black has another chance to close out the game with 23...Be4+ 24.Kf1 Bf5!, threatening an unstoppable mate. This line provides an excellent illustration of the concept of redeployment. Black brings his bishop back along the h1-a8 diagonal to keep White's king out of h1. He then switches to a different diagonal to attack from another angle and force mate. White has no defense (25.Qe2 Bh3+ 26.Ke1 Rg1#).
23...Bh3+ 24.Kh1 Bxf2 (D)
In return for the sacrificed queen, look at the havoc Morphy is causing with his rooks and bishops! Even a cursory glance at the board shows that White's poor king has been abandoned by his army. Black's pieces are all working together in harmony and are moving in for the kill. Question: What is Black threatening? Answer: ...Bg2#. There is only one way to stop it. White has to give back the queen.

Question: What should Black do next? Answer: 26...Re1, forcing a trade of rooks. Exchanging when ahead in material is often the winning technique, but is not always the best option. Sometimes you can win more efficiently by retaining more active pieces. Black's rooks are clearly more active. The best plan in this position is for the e8-rook to penetrate to the seventh rank.
26...Re2 (D)
A rook on the seventh rank traps a king that is on its back rank. This game is now over.
