LESSON 10
[diagram]
THREE FOR THE LADY
n important chess concept ismaterial imbalance. Some examples are rook and pawn versus bishop and knight, or (as in the following game) three minor pieces versus a queen. In both scenarios, the point count is even, but one must consider the relative values of all the pieces involved. This judgment may change at different stages of the game.
S. WEERAMANTRY - M. SCHLAGENHAUF
Harrisburg (Pennsylvania), 1977
1.e4 d6 2.d4 Nf6 3.Nc3 g6 (D)
Question: We have already discussed the Pirc Austrian Attack formation with 4.f4.6 What are some other systems that White can employ against the Pirc? Answer: 4.Nf3. The Classical System. This is the most solid formation for White, quite unlike the unbridled aggression of the Austrian Attack. White is content with a modest two-pawn center (e4, d4). He places his knights on c3 and f3, his light-square bishop on e2, and castles kingside: 4...Bg7 5.Be2
O-O 6.O-O. (D)
After 6.O-O (4.?rf3 variation) After 5.Ne3 (4.f3 variation)
[diagram]
This system is a favorite of conservative players who prefer a quiet opening. Question: What other systems can White use? Answer: Be3 and Qd2 to attack the fianchettoed bishop. The Be3/Qd2 battery is a popular attacking formation against Black's fianchettoed bishop on g7. If White wishes to employ this setup, he must play f2-f3 before bringing his bishop to e3. Otherwise Black could harass the bishop with ...Ng4. After the moves 4.f3 Bg7 5.Be3 (D), Black should hold off castling in favor of immediate queenside expansion. If not, he will be subjected to a dangerous attack similar to the St. George (or Yugoslav) Attack against the Sicilian Dragon. Black has less counterplay than in the Sicilian because the c-file is closed. His best play at this point is5...c6 6.Qd2 b5 with a complex game. I chose a less popular but equally aggressive line.
4.Bc4 Bg7 5.Qe2 (D)
Question: This is an unusual move. Why does White play Qe2 ? (D) What is going on here? After 5?Ne2 After 8 h8 (5...00 variation)
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Schlagenhauf 1977 (Pirc Defense) Answer: I think White wants to push the e-pawn. Very good. There is no other reason for Qe2 except to support the advance of the e-pawn. The intent is to push the pawn to e5 without allowing a queen trade on dl should Black exchange pawns on e5. This idea works particularly well against players who blindly follow the first five moves of the Pirc formation and castle as a matter of course. Qe2 allows White to continue advancing the pawn with e6, driving a wedge into the heart of Black's position. Let me show you briefly what could happen. Iwon a sharp game against 5...O-O at the 1978 Chess Olympiad in Buenos Aires, where I played first board for Sri Lanka, my native country. This game was one of the shortest in the entire Olympiad - it lasted only twelve moves! My opponent was National Master Miguel Clua Ballague who was playing board one for Andorra. The game continued 6.e5 Nfd7 7.e6 fxe6 8.Bxe6+ (not 8.Qxe6+ as Black can free himself later with a counterattack on the queen) 8...Kh8. (D) Question: White has destroyed part of Black's kingside pawn structure. How can he destroy the rest of it? Answer: Play pawn to h4. That is what I played, but 9.Nf3 is stronger. In our game, Black did not find the best defense and collapsed quickly. After 9.h4 Nc6 10.h5, my idea was to play hxg6 and open the h-file. At the time, neither of us realized that Black could have avoided this with 10...g5!. The game continued with 10...Nxd4? 11.hxg6. (D) Black can delay mate by giving away several of his pieces, but in the end mate is unstoppable. Let us return to the original game. Black plays one of the two moves that are generally recommended.
5...Nc6 (D)
Black attacks the d4-pawn. This is the most challenging move against Qe2. The other move is 5...c6, when after 6.e5 dxe5 7.dxe5 Nd5 8.Bd2 White still enjoys a slight advantage. Question: After ...Nc6, what is White's best move? Answer: d5. Does that really make sense? You just put your bishop on c4. Why? To attack f7, right? Why do you then want to play d5 ? You are pushing apawn for no other reason than to attack a piece. The long-term consequence of this move is you shut your bishop out of the game. Before making such a move, ask yourself, "Where can the piece go?" If it can move to a better square, a centralized square, do not attack it. In this position, the knight has a good square on e5, so you do not want to attack it. Answer: Nf3. After 6...€jxd4

White attacks Black's f6-knight.

11.O-O-O (D)
The smoke has cleared and it is time to take stock of the position. Question: Which side do you prefer, and why? Answer: I like Black. He has two extra pawns to go along with his queen. Answer: I prefer White. His pieces are more active. Black's rooks are out of the game. There is no definitive answer. The first reaction most people have is to favor the queen. After all, it is the most powerful piece. On the other hand, three minor pieces could run rings around the queen so long as they are well coordinated. One problem is that White cannot anchor these pieces on strong central squares, as Black's pawns can chase them away. If Black succeeds in getting his king out of the center by castling long, itwill allow him to centralize his rooks. Then After 11.. .c6 After 13...Nf5?

11...c6 (D)
Black gets ready to castle long and makes a little extra space for the queen. This move also takes away an important central square from White's minor pieces.
12.Rhe1 Qc7 13.Nd4
White's knight inches closer to Black's king. All of White's pieces are now within striking range.
13...Bf5? (D)
Black is anxious to castle and moves his bishop to clear the way. This is a mistake. The correct defense is extremely difficult for a human to find. Black should play 13...d5, and if White chooses to sacrifice his bishop with 14.Bxd5, he must not recapture: 14...cxd5? 15.Nxd5 Qd6 16.Nb5 with a crushing attack. Instead 14...g5, threatening to trap the h6-bishop with ...Rg6, lets Black maintain the balance. Without question, this is the critical position. Black is now one move away from castling. If he succeeds, he will take over the initiative. Ifelt that the weakness of the f7-pawn would buy me more time to develop my attack. However, Black's king can reach safety if he is willing to give back a little material. My instinct was that now was the right moment to strike, and I kept After 14. db5 searching for a way to break into Black's position. The winning idea hit me like a bolt from the blue! I blinked and looked again, amazed that I was staring at a forced win. Question: What is the move that, after ...Bf5, launches the winning plan? Answer: Bg5, threatening the e7-pawn. You have identified a good point of attack. Losing the e7-pawn would cause Black's defenses to crumble. Moving this pawn opens the diagonal to d8, thus preventing him from castling. Exercise 10-1: If Black meets 14.Bg5 with 14...e6, how should White proceed? So Bg5 works, but there is something more direct, more forceful, and definitely more convincing. Answer: Nd5, sacrificing a knight. Definitely direct and forceful, but is it any good? After 14...cxd5 15.Bxd5, Black seizes the initiative with 15...Qc5. Answer: Wait! After 14...cxd5, 15.Nb5 is better. Then if 15...Qxc4, White wins back the queen and comes out a piece ahead with 16.Nxd6+ Kd7 17.Nxc4. That is clever. Unfortunately, Black can refuse the second sacrifice and play 15...Qd7 instead. Sacrifices that involve non-forcing moves are difficult to calculate because they allow your opponent a wide choice of replies. A defender is not compelled to accept every piece he is offered. After 15...Qd7, White has little to show for the sacrificed knight. 14.Nd5 is incorrect. Both of your answers have focused on attacking the pawn on e7. Look for another point of attack; e7 is not the only vulnerable square. Answer: 14.Ndb5. Yes!
14.Ndb5! (D)
Using a different knight sacrifice wins by force! Now that the d-file is clear, White has a powerful attack against d6. Now we will see pins, forks, skewers, and discovered checks all rolled into one mouth-watering combination.

17...Kc7 18.Rxe7+ (D)
This is the third consecutive capture with check. This is the position I visualized when I played 14.Ndb5!. I did not analyze any further because I judged this as a winning position. Visualizing a sequence of moves is not as difficult as it seems. Your ability will improve with practice. Remember to pay particular attention to pieces that have been captured and to pieces that have changed position. REVIEW
Themes Systems against the Pirc (discussion after 3...g6). Material imbalances and compensation (6.e5; discussion after 11.O-O-O). Pawn roller as an offensive weapon (11.O-O-O). Disadvantage of too many pawns (11.O-O-O). Temporary nature of the initiative (11.O-O-O; discussion after 13... Bf5). Pins, forks, and discovered checks (14.Ndb5). Advice Do not chase a knight with a center pawn if the knight can go to a better central square. Minor pieces are effective against the queen when they are coordinated. Pawns are an asset when they can drive your opponent's pieces back or control key squares. They are a liability when they inhibit your development. To strengthen an attack, bring in a new piece, preferably with check. The initiative is a temporary advantage. Do not waste it. Remember, not every sacrifice must be accepted. __________
6 See Lesson 5, One to Remember.
A
LESSON 11
[diagram]
CONNECT-THE-DOTS
better pawn structure can give you a lasting initiative and dictate the course of action. In this game, once his pawns are doubled on the g-file, Black's kingside pawns lose their flexibility. White's control of the ...e7-e5 break makes it impossible for his kingside initiative to be challenged and makes the flank attack more powerful. The outcome of this game, played against National Master Larry Tamarkin, is a reminder that chess is not just a cerebral activity, but a sporting contest as well. Iwas in a clearly winning position, but drifted into time pressure and lost on time.
S. WEERAMANTRY - L. TAMARKIN
New York, 1991
1.e4 c6 2.d4 d5 3.exd5 cxd5
This is the Exchange Variation of the Caro-Kann. There are many different variations, but you do not need to study them all just yet. You need only one line against each opening. You can always add others later.
4.Bd3 (D)
White positions his bishop on the long diagonal to h7, with an eye towards a future kingside attack. White's queen and bishop now control g4 and f5 respectively, taking away the natural After 6.Nf4 Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) developing squares from Black's light-square bishop.
4...Nc6
Question: What is the obvious threat? Answer: To take the pawn on d4. Question: Of course. There is also a less obvious threat. What is that? Answer: Play ...Nb4 and trade the knight for White's bishop. Good. Black would love to trade off this powerful bishop. Question: Does White have a move to handle both threats? Answer: Push his pawn to c3. Correct. This builds a pawn chain and stops ...Nb4. Question: Now that we have seen the strength of c3, is there any disadvantage to this move? Answer: Yes. It takes away the c3 square from White's knight. Right. However, the positive outweighs the negative. Moves are seldom totally good or totally bad. You have to weigh the advantages and disadvantages.
5.c3 Nf6 6.Bf4 (D)
White prevents the ...e7-e5 break. Question: A general opening principle states: develop knights before bishops. Can you think of any reason why this principle is widely followed? Answer: Knights are short-range pieces, so they should be moved out early. Is it not true that you would get the knights out soon enough even if you took an extra move or two to first develop the bishops? Answer: Knights can attack center pawns. Bishops cannot. I must admit, I never thought of it that way. I suppose a bishop would have trouble attacking acenter pawn without getting captured itself! However, that is not the reason for the principle. There is one logical square for each knight in the opening - on the third rank and towards the center. Other squares are less desirable. The reason for the principle "knights before bishops" is that it is generally clear where the knight should go. It is not always clear which square is best for a bishop - it has more choices. Therefore, it would make more sense to wait and see how your opponent develops his pieces before deciding where to place your bishop. In playing Bf4, White is clearly violating this principle. Do not take any principles for granted. Examine each and every position on its own merits. There are two words that you should be wary of - one is ALWAYS, and the other is NEVER. Knowing when a principle does not apply is the mark of a good player. Here the justification for moving the dark-square bishop before either knight is quite clear.
6.Nd2 would simply block in that bishop which is needed to guard e5. The immediate 6.Nf3
would also not be successful as it allows Black to set up an annoying pin with ...Bg4.
6...Bg4 (D)
Black decides to play ...Bg4 anyway, in order to attack White's queen. An interesting alternative is to fianchetto the king's bishop with 6...g6 and 7...Bg7. Question: Blocking the attack on his queen with Nf3 would clearly ruin White's earlier strategy. How else can he handle the attack? Answer: Be2. No. There is no logic behind this move. Question: There is one overpowering reason why White should not play Be2. What is it? Answer: You have already developed the bishop to d3. Why move it back? Exactly. This bishop is on a great square. Do not retreat a developed piece without good reason. Question: Give me another suggestion that handles Black's attack. Answer: f3. Question: There is a drawback to this move. What is it? Answer: It takes the f3 square away from the knight. Question: Yes. Can you find a better move? After 10.Se5 (7".Qd7 variation) Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) Answer: Qc2. Question: The queen-and-bishop battery on the long diagonal is strong. However, there is an even better move. What is it? Answer: Qb3. Finally!
7.Qb3 (D)
7.Qb3 seizes the initiative by threatening the b7-pawn. This is the square that was weakened
when Black's bishop went to g4 on the previous move. Black cannot ignore this attack. Question: How should Black handle the attack on the b7-pawn? Answer: Push it up to b6. No. That weakens the light squares unnecessarily. Answer: Defend it with ...Qd7. This is quite playable. However, Black has to be careful not to fall into a trap: 7...Qd7 8.Nd2 e6 9.Ngf3 Be7 (9...Bd6 is better) 10.Ne5. (D) Question: Now, should Black capture the knight or retreat his queen? Answer: Take the knight. Oops. You just fell into the trap. Exercise 11-1a: After 7...Qd7 8.Nd2 e6 9.Ngf3 Be7 10.Ne5 Nxe5, what is the trap that Black has fallen into? Exercise 11-1b: What should Black do instead? Back to the game. Defending the b7-pawn with the queen is the right idea, but not from d7.
7...Qc8 (D)
Question: Can you guess the next few moves for each side? Answer: White should bring out both knights and then castle. Black should play...e6 and ...Be7. Isn't this simple? Both sides are making natural developing moves. Chess is easy when you pay attention to the fundamentals! Your answer is exactly how the game proceeded:
8.Nd2 e6 9.Ngf3 Be7 10.O-O
To this point, both Tamarkin and I have been following a well-trodden path. The nature of chess competition has changed dramatically over the years. A sound knowledge of opening theory is essential if you are to succeed at the master level in modern tournament play. Much of the work is done before ever coming to the board.
10...Bh5 (D)
After 12.Ne5 (10...O-O variation) Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) The idea behind playing ...Bh5 is to play ...Bg6 and neutralize White's pressure along the b1- h7 diagonal. Castling before playing ...Bh5 is less effective: 10...O-O 11.Qc2 Bh5 12.Ne5. (D) Taking the knight now would cost Black a pawn: 12...Nxe5 13.dxe5 Nd7 14.Bxh7+. With White's knight on e5, opposing bishops with 12... Bg6 will allow White to trade knight for bishop, rather than exchanging bishops. By playing ...Bh5 before castling, Black threatens to place his bishop on g6 before White plays Ne5. Should White attempt to rush Ne5, he would be saddled with an inferior pawn structure:
10...O-O 11.Ne5 Nxe5 12.dxe5 Nd7. (D)
Black now has c5 available for his knight, and he can still oppose bishops with ...Bh5-g6. Small differences in move order can have a profound effect on a position.
11.Qc2 Bg6 12.Bxg6 (D)
After 12...fxg6 (variation)
[diagram]
The trade of bishops cannot be avoided. White prefers to initiate the bishop trade in order to double Black's pawns. Question: Which way should Black recapture and why? Answer: With the h-pawn, to give the rook an open file. Question: Good. What about the other option? Answer: Capturing with the f-pawn will break up Black's pawn structure and leave him with a backward pawn on e6. Yes. It is a question of pawn structure. (D) Every time pawns break up into small groups, we refer to each group as a pawn island. The more pawn islands there are, the weaker the pawn structure. Certain squares become difficult to defend. If Black recaptures with the f-pawn, his pawns will break up into three islands. The base of the pawn chain will shift from f7 to e6, making the e6-pawn backward and vulnerable because it is situated on White's half-open e-file. On the other hand, ifBlack recaptures with the h-pawn, then f7 provides a solid foundation for the entire structure.
12...hxg6 13.Rae1 (D)
After 13.aael
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) Question: Why should White play Rae1 when he could just as easily move the other rook over? How do you decide which rook to activate when there is only one open file? Answer: The a1-rook is not doing anything on the other side of the board. Question: True, but what about the other rook? Is it any better on f1? How is this rook going to participate in the game? Answer: You could double rooks on the e-file. If your intention is to double rooks, it makes no difference which rook you move first. Furthermore, doubling rooks is most effective when targeting a backward pawn. Doubling in this position is the wrong strategy because there is no target. Question: When I played 13.Rae1, I already had a plan for activating the f1-rook. What was my plan? Answer: To move your bishop and knight out of the way, and push the f-pawn. Excellent! Imagine for a moment that the bishop and knight have moved off the f-file and I have advanced my pawn to f4. Both rooks are now active. In the early middlegame, rooks belong on open files or behind advancing pawns.
13...O-O (D)
Now the opening is over. Both sides are fully developed. At this stage of the game, finding the appropriate long-term plan is critical. Plans do not just appear out of thin air - the pawn structure dictates the plan. Connect-the-dots The pawns establish the boundary of your territory. Your entire middlegame plan can be based upon how these boundaries are drawn. To illustrate this, let's play connect-the-dots. If these dots represent the pawns, you can see the board is divided into two triangles. White's pawn chain is pointing towards the kingside and he is clearly controlling more space in that sector. It will be difficult for Black to challenge this superiority without bringing more pieces over from his queenside. However, Black's pawn structure makes this difficult because the pawn on e6 restricts the flow from one side to the other. He has a very narrow funnel to move his pieces through. Build your attack in the sector where you have more space. If you understand this structure, you will appreciate that White has a natural kingside attack with a high probability of success. You will also realize the importance of preventing the move ...e6-e5. If Black cannot advance his pawn to e5, he will be unable to free his position. Look at the pieces White has concentrated on that square - a pawn, a rook, a knight, and a bishop. Black's pawn is not going anywhere! A remarkable feature about this position is that not one of Black's five kingside pawns can move without serious consequences. The g6-pawn cannot move without endangering the king; the pawn behind it cannot move at all; the e6-pawn is frozen in its tracks; the d5-pawn is blockaded; and if the f7-pawn moves, the entire kingside pawn structure will collapse! Once you determine that the pawn structure is not going to change, you can organize your attack more effectively. A methodical approach is essential. Start with a concept, then consider specifics. The danger of not following this order is that you will make random moves, and never put together an organized attack. First, decide which squares your pieces should occupy in order to maximize their attacking potential. Second, determine how to get your pieces to the ideal squares. Next, establish whether or not your opponent can prevent your pieces from reaching the preferred destinations. Finally, consider whether or not your opponent can ignore your plan completely and launch a counterattack of his own. If you think through these four steps and are satisfied that your ideas are valid, then go ahead with your plan. After 13...O-O
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) Now, let's apply this process to the game position. (D) Step One... Question: Where should White place his pieces for an attack on the king? Answer: I like one knight on g5, and the other on f3. Question: That certainly brings the knights into the attack. What about the other pieces? Answer: I like a rook on h3 and the queen on h4, threatening mate on h8. Excellent. Even if this does not lead to mate, an invasion on the h-file will present many problems for Black. Question: How about the pawns? Will they be used in the attack? Answer: No. Do not push the h-pawn. It will only block the file for the queen and rook. Good. The h-file must be kept clear. What about the other kingside pawns? We have already noted that f4 is a desirable move. It not only frees the f1-rook, but also helps to control the e5 square. As for the g2-pawn, it could go to g4 to keep Black's knight out of h5. Step Two... Question: Do our pieces have a clear path to these squares? Answer: Yes. The f3-knight can go directly to g5 and the d2-knight can then go to f3. Answer: The e1-rook can go to e3, then h3. Answer: The queen can go to d3, then g3, then h4. Excellent. And we can play f4 once we move the bishop. That was easy! Step Three - can Black stop these moves? Obviously, he can try to keep the knight out of g5 by playing ...f6, but that would destroy his kingside pawn structure. He cannot prevent the d2-knight from going to f3. And, there is no reasonable way for him to attack the squares the rook will be using. That just leaves the queen. Question: Can Black stop the queen from occupying h4? Answer. Yes, because his bishop on e7 can attack h4. Question: True. Can we counter that? After 14...£sh5 Answer: Yes. That's why we want our knight on g5. Actually, the knight would be very useful on e5. We could put our bishop on g5 instead, and set up a trade of bishops to secure h4 for the queen. A key feature of this position is that Black's kingside pawn structure is fixed, thus closing the center. As a result, White's plan to move his pieces over to the flank cannot easily be disrupted. Finally, Step Four. Question: Can Black ignore White's plans and mount his own attack? Answer: Yes. He may be able to attack down the c-file. Thank you! This is his half-open file which is the logical place to look for play. Question: Which pieces are sitting on that file? Answer: The two opposing queens. Question: Right. If you see that, you will notice a tactic Black could use. Answer: He can play ...Nxd4 if White moves his f3-knight. Do you see the value of using this process? You need to follow all these steps. It is not enough to focus on your moves alone. When you play chess, you are playing two games in your head: the moves you can make, and the moves your opponent can make. Failing to anticipate your opponent's counterplay will ruin your own plans.
14.Qd3
White moves his queen off the c-file and inches it towards h4.
14...Nh5 (D)
Black is playing on the kingside, but this is not a good strategy. White's attack is too strong. Instead, Black should consider moving his f-rook away to create an escape route for his king. It is very hard to plan an active defense because Black's kingside pawns cannot move. In similar positions, acommon strategy is to expand on the queenside with ...b7-b5, ...a7- a5, and ...b5-b4, then initiate a trade of pawns on c3. This trade would saddle White with a backward pawn, which would then become a target for Black. This strategy is called a minority attack - two pawns are attacking three. Here, unfortunately, such a plan would be too slow compared to White's direct assault on the king. After 15±g5
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann)
15.Bg5 (D)
White moves his bishop out of danger and offers a trade of bishops. Should Black accept, White would recapture with his knight, paving the way for the pawn to advance to f4.
15...Qd8
Black sets up a battery attacking the bishop. Question: How should White handle the attack without changing his plan? Answer: Capture the bishop. No. It is better to support the bishop with Qe3 and let Black make the trade.
16.Qe3 Bxg5 17.Nxg5 (D)
White recaptures with his knight. By having Black initiate the trade, White has gained a tempo. Meanwhile, his queen keeps inching towards h4.
17...Qf6?
A careless move. Black probably intended 18...Qf4 to set up a queen trade, hoping to turn back the attack.
18.f4 (D)
After 18,f4 After 19.g4 Finally! So much for ...Qf4. White's pawns on d4 and f4 now have a stranglehold on the e5 square. With the center firmly under control, White's flank attack will be more difficult to repel. Question: What is the immediate threat? Answer: g4, trapping the knight. Yes. The only way to save it is to move the queen, creating a retreat for the knight.
18...Qe7 19.g4 (D)
This move appears to violate another important principle, in this case a middlegame principle: do not push the pawns in front of your castled king. Question: Under what circumstances might you be justified in ignoring this principle? Answer: When your opponent has no counterplay against your king. Question: Correct. But what would stop your opponent's counterplay? Answer: A closed center. Exactly. When the center is fluid, do not move the pawns in front of your castled king. When the center is closed, they are free to move.
19...Nf6 20.Qh3 Rfc8 (D)
After Z0,..Rfc8 After 21...Sd6 Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann) Black intends to run his king to f8 and block a back-rank check with ...Ng8.
21.Rf3
The f-rook is heading to the h-file. Using the e-rook would have been more accurate so that the f4-pawn would remain protected.
21...Qd6 (D)
This is where I lost the game. I took fifteen minutes to make my next move, even though the time control was game in thirty! There really was no need to spend so much time, because I had already seen a beautiful combination. Question: Do you see it? Answer: The queen checks on h8, setting up a knight fork on f7. Absolutely correct. Isn't this a pretty move? How many chances do you get to stick your queen right in front of the enemy king? This is an example of a magnet combination attracting Black's king into a fork. There was no need to look for something better, because this move leads to the win of a pawn and destroys Black's kingside pawn structure. Unfortunately, I was reluctant to trade queens and kept searching for a checkmate.
22.Qh8+ Kxh8 23.Nxf7+ Kg8 24.Nxd6 (D)
After 24.Nxd6 White is ahead in material and has the superior pawn structure.
24...Rc7 25.Rxe6 Rf8
Black decided not to take the g4-pawn because White would reply with Rg3, skewering the knight to the g6-pawn.
26.h3 (D)
White is up two pawns and should win easily. He simply has to consolidate by doubling his rooks and improving the position of his other knight. The wasted time on move 22 left me with only moments on the clock and we were playing without a delay. I did manage to make ten or more additional moves from this position, but my opponent hung on three pawns down in the endgame, and my flag fell! After this unhappy experience, I can give you the following advice: If you see a position where you are clearly winning, there is no need to search for the perfect move. After 10...Qxe5? (7,..Bd7 variation) Weeramantry - Tamarkin 1991 (Caro-Kann)
ANSWERS
Exercise 11-1a: After 7...Qd7 8.Nd2 e6 9.Ngf3 Be7 10.Ne5 Nxe5? (D), what is the trap that Black has fallen into? Answer: 11.dxe5 wins material. Black's knight is under attack, but should it take the time to move away, 12.Bb5 would pin Black's queen to his king. Exercise 11-1b: What should Black do instead? Answer: Instead of 10...Nxe5, Black can survive by retreating his queen with 10...Qc8. This loses time, but at least it does not cost him any material.
REVIEW
Themes Controlling the central pawn break (6.Bf4). Developing knights before bishops (discussion after 6.Bf4). Proper timing of moves (6.Bf4 vs. 6.Nf3 and 10...Bh5 vs. 10...O-O) Whether or not to initiate a trade (12.Bxg6 and 15...Qd8 16.Qe3). Pawn islands (12...fxg6 variation). Choosing which rook to place on a file (13.Rae1). The relationship between pawn structure and space (discussion after 13...O-O). General planning in the middlegame (discussion after 13...O-O). Minority attack (discussion after 14...Nh5). Advancing pawns in front of the castled king (19.g4). Magnet combination (discussion before 22.Qh8+). Advice Moves are seldom totally good or totally bad. You have to weigh the advantages and disadvantages. There are two words that you should be wary of - one is ALWAYS, and the other is
NEVER.
Do not retreat a developed piece without good reason. Small differences in move order can have a profound effect on a position. The more pawn islands there are, the weaker the pawn structure. In the early middlegame, rooks belong on open files or behind advancing pawns. Build your attack in the sector where you have more space. Start with a concept, then consider specifics. Failing to anticipate your opponent's counterplay will ruin your own plans. When the center is fluid, do not move the pawns in front of your castled king. When the center is closed, they are free to move. If you see a position where you are clearly winning, there is no need to search for the perfect move. Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack)

IT'S NEVER OVER
ou may tend to think of drawn games as dull games, but this is not always the case. Iam going to show you a game I drew that was far from dull. It is a good example of "chess as a struggle." The initiative shifts back and forth between myself and my opponent, National Master Juan Carlos Urbaneja Navarro, a leading player from Venezuela.
WEERAMANTRY - URBANEJA
New York, 1991
1.e4 c5 2.Nc3 Nc6 3.f4
Another Grand Prix Attack. We first looked at this opening in Lesson 7 in my game against Mitchell Goldberg. Do you know how this opening got its name? It was used frequently by British players in the Cutty Sark Grand Prix tournaments in England in the 1970s. The inspiration, however, were the games of the Danish GM Bent Larsen in the 1960s, but he liked to play f2-f4 on move 2.
3...g6
The fianchetto formation is a popular alternative to 3...e6.7
4.Nf3 (D)
After 4".e6 (variation) After 7.Qxd4 (4...e6 variation) A common mistake in this position is for Black to reply with 4...e6. (D) Question: Why is 4...e6 a mistake? You do not have to look for an exact sequence. Think back to previous lectures and give me a general answer. Answer: It leaves holes on the dark squares. Good. I am glad you remembered about color weaknesses. By playing both ...e7-e6 and ...g7- g6, Black opens up two diagonals, but has only one dark-square bishop. He is creating weaknesses that cannot be controlled. As the game goes on, and Black gets better piece development, he may be able to cover some of the weak squares with other pieces. At this stage of the game, however, he is not in a position to do that. Question: How do you prove that 4...e6 is bad? Answer: Play 5.d4 cxd4 6.Nxd4 Nxd4 7.Qxd4 (D), preventing Black's bishop from going to g7. Your analysis is correct. In order to take advantage of weaknesses like this, you must open the position, and thus be able to target the dark squares. Instead of trading knights, Black would do better to play 6... Bg7. (D) After 7.Ndb5 (4...e6 variation) Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Question: How should White proceed? Answer: Push e5. That will help White control the dark squares. Good. e5 is definitely part of White's plan, but there is a more important move to be played first. Answer: Be3. This is a good developing move, but is too passive. Look for a more active move. Answer: Play Ndb5. (D) Yes! 7.Ndb5 is much stronger than 7.Be3. With Black's bishop already committed to the other diagonal, he cannot stop White's knight from jumping into d6. Then e5 makes a lot more sense because it anchors the knight on an advanced outpost square. Occupying d6 would also bury Black's c8-bishop, giving White a substantial lead in development. All things considered, Black cannot use the move order 4...e6 in this opening. Back to the actual game where Black played the correct move.
4...Bg7 5.Bc4
White's light-square bishop usually goes to c4 or b5. Both moves are reliable. In general, the bishop goes to b5 if Black has moved his d6-pawn, and to c4 if he has not.
5...e6
Now ...e7-e6 is quite playable as White cannot open the position with d2-d4. After 6,f5
[diagram]
After 7..,gxf5 (6,..exf5 variation)
[diagram]
After 7.d3 (6,..gxf5 variation)
[diagram]
6.f5 (D)
This pawn sacrifice is an attempt to seize the initiative by disrupting Black's pawn structure. If Black takes with his e-pawn, White can take back without losing control of the center (6...exf5
7.exf5). It is clear that Black should not capture again with 7...gxf5 (D), because he will be
saddled with doubled and isolated f-pawns where his extra pawn would be of little use. If Black takes with his g-pawn on move 6, then White must continue with 7.d3 (D), keeping his pawn on e4 to prevent Black from playing ...d7-d5. Of course, if Black takes on e4, White will take back with the pawn (7...fxe4 8.dxe4). So long as Black has a pawn on e6, White must maintain a pawn on e4. After 9...Na5 Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Either way, White's superior development gives him excellent compensation for the sacrificed pawn. Back to the game. My opponent did not take the pawn and instead played the move recommended by theory.
6...Nge7 (D)
Black prepares to castle and puts more pressure on the f5 square.
7.fxe6 dxe6
While this move has been played by many grandmasters, playing 7... fxe6, keeping open the possibility of ...d7-d5, is the better move.
8.d3 O-O 9.O-O
We have reached the end of the opening. There are a few pieces left to be developed, but now the fun begins.
9...Na5 (D)
Black wants to trade off the c4-bishop and double White's c-pawns. This plan is inefficient because it involves three knight moves to capture a piece that has moved only once. The extra time needed to carry out this maneuver helps White to complete his development before Black does. Meanwhile, Black's bishop is still languishing on c8, impeding the coordination of his rooks. After 10,Qel
[diagram]
After ll.dxc4 (10,,,Sxc4 variation)
[diagram]
10.Qe1 (D)
White uses the dark squares to transfer his queen to the kingside, a common plan when attacking the fianchetto. This move also avoids a queen trade should White have to recapture on c4 with his pawn. Black could continue with his plan of trading knight for bishop, but, as we will see, the doubled c-pawns actually help White, for example 10...Nxc4 11.dxc4. (D) White's c4-pawn now controls d5. As a result, his e-pawn is free to advance to e5. This clears the e4 square for his knight and strengthens the kingside attack. In addition, White will be able to use the d-file for his rook and increase his control of the center. Question: What is the best placement for White's pieces to conduct a kingside attack? Answer: Play Qh4, then Bh6. Yes. The queen belongs on h4 from where it controls the key dark squares h6 and g5. White can decide whether to place his bishop on g5 or continue on to h6 keeping g5 open for the f3- knight.

After 12.Ng5
[diagram]
Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Black challenges White's queen, hoping to dislodge it from h4. White cannot retreat his queen because 12.Qh3 will be met with the discovered attack 12...e5. However, White has a surprising move. Question: Can you find it? Answer: Bg5. If Black takes the bishop, the knight will take back, threatening Qh7#. Bravo!
12.Bg5 (D)
Amazingly, the h6-pawn does not prevent White from playing this move. The first time I used this idea was against International Master Dr. Hans Berliner, in the
1975 Eastern Open. Dr. Berliner was a former World Correspondence Champion best known as
the programmer of HiTech, which was once the strongest computer chess program in the world. If the queen moves away, then Bxh6 shatters Black's defenses and opens g5 for White's knight. Remember the squares h4, h6, and g5. They are the key to the dark-square attack against Black's fianchetto. At this point, I was walking around confident that I had this game already won. The problem with chess is, it's never over! After forty-five minutes' thought, Urbaneja came up with an ingenious defense. Question: What is Black's clever defensive maneuver? After 13...*fd4+ Answer: ...f6. In some ways this does stop the attack, but it creates a new weakness on g6. After Bxh6, Black's position is collapsing. This is a tough sequence to find, so I will give you a clue. The only way to survive the attack is for Black to get his queen to g7. My opponent just sat there until he figured it out. Icould not believe it! Answer: 12...Bd4+ 13.Nxd4. Hold on. White is not going to take the bishop. He would just play Kh1 instead. But you are right that the d4 square has something to do with it. Time for another hint. Question: What is the one move we are all assuming Black cannot play? Answer: ...hxg5. Of course. But that is a wrong assumption!

14.Kh1

14...Bh6 saves the day. Unbelievable! Black sacrifices his bishop to open the long diagonal
for his queen.
15.Qxh6 Qg7
End of attack. Black suffered a little damage, but he was able to regroup and defend. Now the attacker must retreat and reorganize before starting again.
16.Qh4
White does not want the queen on h3 where it would be exposed to a discovered attack from the c8-bishop. Still, it is not clear how White should continue the attack. I have a sad story to tell. This is not the only time I have had this position. A little more than a year after this game was played, I got to the same position in a thirty-minute game against five- time U.S. champion Grandmaster Gata Kamsky. Kamsky played 16...Ne5. (D) I failed to find the best continuation and ended up losing. The consensus in the postmortem analysis was that I had excellent chances had I played Rf6!. Back to the game.

Question: Why does ...Ne5 make sense? Answer: It attacks a pawn. It attacks the c4-pawn and centralizes the knight. What else can you tell me about the e5 square? Answer: It is an outpost. Remember, the square in front of an isolated pawn is a great outpost. Put a homing device on that square and jump right in there.
18.Rad1
White is answering the attack on the c4-pawn with a counterattack. This developing move sets up an indirect defense to the threat of ...Nxc4. (D) Exercise 12-2: If Black tries 18...Nxc4, how does White respond?
18...f6 19.Nf3 Nxc4
Black has re-established material equality, but here comes the second wave of the attack.
20.e5! (D)
After 21,£te4 Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Advancing the pawn sets up a discovered attack on Black's knight. If Black accepts this pawn sacrifice with 20...Nxe5 21.Nxe5 fxe5, White will either win Black's queen or deliver a forced mate. Exercise 12-3: After 20...Nxe5 21.Nxe5 fxe5, how should White continue? Of course, Black did not take the pawn.
20...Ne3
A more challenging move, Black forks White's rooks. This was not unexpected.
21.Ne4 (D)
Now you see the real point of 20.e5. It is a clearance move. If the knight reaches f6, White's attack becomes unstoppable. His last piece will have entered the fray with devastating effect. Meanwhile, Black can take only one rook at a time. Why bother trying to save either one? Leave them there and let him have his pick! Question: If Black plays 21...Nxf1, how should White reply? Answer: Just play Nxf6+. Good. This is stronger than recapturing on f1. What happens now? Answer: Black plays ...Kf7, then White checks with the other knight. Yes. 22...Kf7 23.Ng5+ Ke7 24.Qc4 gives White an overwhelming attack. After 23,.. xf8
[diagram]
Question: But, what if Black decides to return material with 22...Rxf6 instead of moving his king? Answer: White will take back with his pawn, attacking the queen. Right. Question: After 23.exf6 Qc7, how does White continue? Answer: 24.Ng5. Excellent. Black cannot stop all of White's threats. Now if Black instead takes the other rook with 21...Nxd1, White will again play 22.Nxf6+ Rxf6 23.exf6. After 23...Qh7, White simply trades down into an endgame where he can force the promotion of his f-pawn: 24.Qxh7+ Kxh7 25.Rxd1 g5 26.Ne5. Black did not take either rook. Instead, he counterattacked with...
21...g5
Now both rooks are hanging, as is the queen! In situations like this, forget about retreating anything. Press on with the attack and sacrifice the next piece.
22.Nfxg5 fxg5 23.Rxf8+ Kxf8 (D)
Unfortunately, all these moves had taken me a very long time, and my flag was hanging! I had a minute left to get to move thirty. Back then, we did not have the luxury of increment or delay.

This is also a losing move. Black should play 29...Qxe5 instead.

One mistake after another! 30.Qh3 wins easily. There is no excuse for trading this powerful knight for a bishop that has not even moved. But this was the last move before time control, and After 34...Qdl+ I just could not think of anything else.
30...Qf6 (D)
I was so relieved to have reached time control that at first I did not realize the danger I was in. The position has changed, and Black has taken over the initiative. White's king on h1 is in more danger than Black's king on g6. White can no longer protect the rook and f1 at the same time.
31.Qd4 Rxc8
This rook sacrifice deflects White's rook from guarding d1. Black can now force a perpetual check.

ANSWERS
Exercise 12-1: Which move, 14.Kh1 or 14.Rf2, gives White better winning chances? Answer: White has better winning chances with 14.Rf2!. (D) This rook will not stay pinned once Black transfers his queen to g7 for defense: 14...Bh6
15.Qxh6 Qg7 16.Qh3. (D)
By playing 14.Rf2 instead of 14.Kh1, White saves a valuable tempo and can more quickly double his rooks on the f-file. Exercise 12-2: If Black tries 18...Nxc4, how does White respond? (D) After 23.e5 (18,..Cixc4 variation) Answer: White launches a counterattack with 19.Nxf7. Now 19...Rxf7 20.Rd8+ Rf8 21.Rfxf8+ Qxf8 22.Rxf8+ Kxf8 23.e5. (D) Black cannot capture this pawn, as 23...Nxe5 loses the knight to 24.Qh8+. White has a material advantage as well as a commanding lead in development. Exercise 12-3: After 20...Nxe5 21.Nxe5 fxe5 (D), how should White continue? Answer: White wins with 22.Rxf8+: 1) 22...Qxf8 is met by 23.Rd8 Bd7 24.Rxf8+ winning the queen. 2) 22...Kxf8 leads to a forced mate in 5: 23.Qd8+ Kf7 24.Rf1+ Qf6 25.Rxf6+ Kg7 26.Qf8+ Kh7 27.Rf7#. Exercise 12-4: Find White's winning line after 28...Nc6. (D) After 28...&C6
[diagram]
After 36...Sb£3 (36.@g2 variation) Weeramantry - Urbaneja 1991 (Sicilian Grand Prix Attack) Answer: White can win with 29.Qd3+: 1) If 29...Kh6, White has a forced mate in 5 or less. For example: 30.Nf7+ Kh5 31.Rh8+ Qxh8 32.Nxh8 g4 33.Qg6+ Kh4 34.Qh6#. 2) If 29...Kh5, then 30.Qh3+ Kg6 31.g4 Qh7 32.Qf3 Nxe5 33.Qe4+ Kh6 34.Qxe5 with a crushing attack. Exercise 12-5: Instead of offering a draw, Black could have met 36.Qg2 with 36...Nf3 (D) threatening ...Qd1+. Why not? Answer: White turns the game around with the surprising reply 37.Rd8!, after which 37...Qxd8
38.Qxf3 leaves White a pawn ahead.
REVIEW
Themes Weak color complex (4...e6 variation). Pawn sacrifice for initiative (6.f5 gxf5 7.d3). Pawn sacrifice for structural advantage (6.f5 exf5 7.exf5 gxf5). Attacking the dark squares around Black's castled king in a fianchetto formation (10.Qe1;
11.Qh4; 12.Bg5).
Accepting doubled pawns to control central squares (10...Nxc4 variation). Reorganizing the attack (16.Qh4 through 20.e5). Square clearance (20.e5). Advice Open the position to exploit weak squares. Advance a pawn to clear a square for a piece. Try approaching a target from different angles. Whenever possible, defend actively and counterattack. Sacrifice to gain the initiative. Sacrifice again to maintain it when necessary. When two pieces of the same value are attacked, it may be a waste of time to move either one - your opponent can only capture one piece at a time. Manage your time efficiently. Serious time trouble can spell disaster. __________
7 See Lesson 7, Strike While the Knight Is Cold.

18.d5!
This is a multi-purpose clearance sacrifice. In one move, White takes away d5 from Black's knight and frees d4 for his own piece.
18...exd5 19.Nd4 Qa6 20.Kb1 Bd6 21.Qf3
Rather than trade on d6 himself, White protects his f4-bishop and invites Black to make the trade.
21...Bxf4 22.Qxf4 Ne5 23.Qf5+ Kb8 (D)
White is in complete control of the game, primarily because Black's pawns are fractured and lack mobility. The extent to which this affects the outcome becomes clearer as the game progresses.
24.f4
Although Black's knight is placed well on e5, he is unable to maintain its centralized position. White's knight, on the other hand, has a permanent home on d4. After 29...Bc5
24...Nd7 25.Qxd5 Ne5 26.Qe4 Ng4
Black threatens to fork all of White's major pieces with ...Ng4-f2, but the knight is unable to receive any assistance from the rest of Black's pieces.
27.Qe2 Qb6 28.c3 f5 29.Rhe1
There is no hurry to capture Black's weak pawn on f5. This structural weakness will not disappear. Activating one's pieces takes priority.
29...Qc5 (D)
Black momentarily protects his f5-pawn, but can do little to contest White's control of the e- file.
30.Qe7 Qxe7 31.Rxe7
The trade of queens further highlights Black's structural weaknesses.
31...Rhe8 32.Rde1 Rxe7 33.Rxe7 Nf6 34.Rxf7 Nxh5 35.Nxf5
The recent flurry of captures has clarified the position. White has an extra pawn and a clear 2-1 pawn majority on the kingside. His rook has penetrated to the seventh rank, and his knight is more centralized.
35...Rd3 36.Rf8+ Kc7 37.Rg8 Kd7 38.Rg6 b5 (D)

This strategy of handling the minority attack was popularized by former world champion José Raúl Capablanca. It is effective as long as the weakness of the backward pawn on c3 cannot be exploited.

As Black cannot challenge White's control of the h-file, he uses his knights to hold White's queen and rook at bay. However, this move is a mistake. Instead, he could have shut White down with 29...Ke7 30.Qxg7 Nf6 31.Rh3 Qf8. Black's control of the a-file gives him adequate compensation for the pawn.
30.Rf3
This is a good idea, but the timing is wrong as it gives Black's a7-rook a chance to leave his second rank with check. White can maintain complete control by leaving this rook on f1 and playing 30.Rh3 instead.

After 32.Rfh3 (D7) Weeramantry - Walton 1986 If this were not check, White would now fork Black's king and queen with Nd7+.
31.Kg2 R8a7?
The decisive mistake. Black must protect d7 with his queen, not his rook. The a8-rook must stay guarding his first rank. Instead, Black has a remarkable defense in 31...Qd6. If White pursues his idea of tripling on the h-file in order to threaten mate by sacrificing his queen on g8, Black will simply give up the knight and walk away: 32.Rfh3 Qxf4 33.Qxg8+ Ke7. (D) Although Black is down a piece, he has enough counterplay to hold the balance. If White continues his attack by doubling on the eighth rank, Black moves his knight to c7, protecting the a8-rook and discovering an attack on White's queen: 34.Rh8 Nc7. Once the queen moves away, Black's a8-rook pins the e2-bishop with ...R8a2 and helps recover the piece. Back to the game.
32.Rfh3 (D)
White triples on the h-file and threatens Qxg8+. Black is helpless.
32...Nef6 33.Qxg7+ Ke8 34.Qxf6
This pseudo-sacrifice of the queen seals the victory. If the queen is captured with 34...Nxf6, White skewers Black's king and queen with 35.Rh8+ and comes out ahead a piece.

1.e4 e5 2.Nf3 d6 3.d4 exd4 4.Qxd4 Nc6 5.Bb5 Bd7 6.Bxc6 Bxc6 7.Nc3 Nf6 8.O-O Be7
9.Nd5 Bxd5 10.exd5 O-O 11.Bg5 (D)
White has secured a considerable advantage in space. His pieces are occupying five ranks, while Black is confined to three. The extra space gives White's pieces greater mobility. The difference in the two bishops is particularly striking.
11...c6
Black attempts to break this stranglehold by changing the pawn structure. A better option is to attack the bishop with 11...h6, setting up a simple combination should the bishop retreat to h4: 12.Bh4 Nxd5, winning a pawn.
12.c4
White fully intends to maintain a pawn on d5, preventing Black from activating his bishop along the a3-f8 diagonal.
12...cxd5 13.cxd5 Re8 14.Rfe1
White takes the first step in activating his rooks: occupy an open file.
14...a5
Black moves the a-pawn to free his rook from defensive duty, but moving it one space forward to a6 is preferable as the pawns would stay connected.
15.Re2 Rc8?
A mistake. Black should remove any possibility of a back-rank mate by playing 15...h6.
16.Rae1
The second step is now complete: White doubles his rooks on the open file. Black is now in grave danger. If White captures on f6, Black will be forced to take back with his g-pawn, exposing his king.
16...Qd7 (D)
(D8) Adams - Torre 1920 Black moves the queen off the back rank to coordinate his rooks and guard against the immediate threat. However, Black's back-rank weakness still remains.
17.Bxf6
This initiates a winning combination featuring a series of queen offers based on the threat of a back-rank mate.
17...Bxf6
Black has an interesting alternative in 17...gxf6. Exercise D8-1: If 17...gxf6, White can try to win material with 18.Rxe7. Does this work?
18.Qg4!
White offers his queen in an attempt to deflect Black's queen away from its protection of e8.
18...Qb5
Naturally, 18...Qxg4 allows a forced mate: 19.Rxe8+ Rxe8 20.Rxe8#.
19.Qc4
A second attempt at deflection.
19...Qd7
Black must maintain his queen on the a4-e8 diagonal.
20.Qc7!
Yet another attempt to force Black's queen off the diagonal. This is the most striking of the three queen offers as White's queen plants itself directly in Black's face. Still, White's queen cannot be touched.
20...Qb5 (D)
Despite White's best efforts, Black's queen still occupies the a4-e8 diagonal. White must find a new idea to avoid repeating moves.
21.a4!
The key move. Exercise D8-2: What happens if White plays 21.Qxb7 in yet another attempt at deflecting Black's queen?
21...Qxa4 22.Re4
This follow-up move is essential. If White resumes his attempts at deflection with 22.Qxa5, Black simply plays 22...Qd7 23.Qc7 Qb5, repeating moves.
22...Qb5 23.Qxb7 (D)

(D9) Nakamura - Fluviá Poyatos 2007 The first impression is that White's king is in serious danger, but a closer examination reveals that Black's threat of ...Qg5+ can be easily parried. Black has three pawns for a bishop, but his pawns are on the queenside and are not useful in attacking White's king.
33.Qb5 Qxd4 34.Qe5
White centralizes his queen and offers a trade. Black has no immediate checks.
34...Qd8
A queen trade is out of the question, for White's bishop pair will easily dominate Black's pawns in an endgame.
35.Rd2
The rook cannot be captured because of White's mate threat on c7.
35...Bd7
Black's pieces are forced to retreat even further.
36.Rd4
This move keeps control of the game and guarantees equality. However, 36.Rxd7 would generate winning chances as White's bishop pair is superior to Black's rook.
36...e6+ (D)
After 39...JU7 Black finally gets in a check. White's king has the choice of advancing or retreating.
37.Kxh5
The king fights back. White is not afraid to capture this pawn; his pieces are well coordinated and can control Black's key attacking squares.
37...Be8+?
The fact that White's king is wide open with no pawn cover mesmerizes Black into believing that he is winning. The more realistic course is to settle for a repetition with 37...Qe8+ 38.Kh4 (38.Kh6 Qf8+) 38... Qd8+ 39.Kh5 Qe8+.
38.Kh6
White's king continues to move up the board. The lack of communication between Black's queen and rook, coupled with White's control of key lines of attack, ensures that the king will be safe.
38...Qe7
Black searches for a checking square...
39.Rf4
...and White calmly shuts him down.
39...Bf7 (D)
Black shields the f8 square and prepares to check White's king.
40.Qf6
With one final move to make before time control, White misses a clear win. Exercise D9-1: After 39...Bf7, find a winning continuation for White.
40...Qf8+ 41.Kh7 Kb8 42.Rf2 e5
Black is probably quite frustrated at not finding a way to checkmate a king that appears to be defenseless. He misses the opportunity to force a draw by repetition, here and on the previous move, with 42...Rg6 43.Bxg6 Bg8+ 44.Kh8 Bf7+. As a result, White now reaps a full point, an apt reward for coordinating his pieces well in defense.

1.e4 c5 2.Nf3 Nc6 3.Bb5 d6 4.O-O Bd7 5.Re1 Nf6 6.c3 a6 7.Bf1 Bg4 8.d4 cxd4 9.cxd4
d5 10.e5 Ng8 11.e6 Bxe6 12.Rxe6 fxe6 (D) White has sacrificed an exchange and a pawn to disrupt Black's pawn structure. He still retains his bishop pair and can target the e6-pawn at the appropriate time. This square is difficult to defend as Black lacks a light-square bishop. The position is dynamically balanced: superior structure versus greater material.
13.Qb3
A multi-purpose move. The immediate point is to make Black choose how to defend the b7- pawn.
13...Na5
Black finds the best reply but has to decentralize his knight. Protecting the pawn with 13...Rb8 or 13...Qc7 gives White a powerful attack with 14.Bf4. As for 13...Qd7, White has the knight maneuver Nc3-a4-c5 which, together with Bf4, exploits Black's weak dark squares on the queenside. Exercise D10-1: In the variation 13...Qd7 14.Nc3, Black can stop Na4 with 14...b5. What then?
14.Qe3
White's queen has gained a tempo on its way to the e-file. With his queen on e3 and Black's knight no longer on c6, White controls the d4 square. If White had played directly to the e-file with 13.Qe2, Black could have put pressure on White's d-pawn with ...g7-g6 and ...Bf8-g7.
14...Rc8 15.Nc3 Qd6 16.g3 g6 17.Bh3
White carries out the most direct plan for applying pressure to e6.
17...Bh6 18.Qe2 Rc6? (D)
After 18...Ec6? Black chooses to defend e6 but places his rook on an awkward square. Black should abandon the pawn and focus on counterplay. To this end, trading bishops immediately with ...Bxc1 will make it easier to play natural developing moves.
19.Bg5!
This gives White a clear advantage, as it hampers the development of Black's g8-knight.
19...Kf8
Trading bishops on g5 will simply grant White quicker access to e6: 19...Bxg5 20.Nxg5 Nf6
21.Nxe6.
20.Re1 Kg7 21.Bxh6+ Nxh6 22.Bxe6
The e6-pawn falls as expected. Furthermore, both black knights are temporarily out of play.
22...Re8
Giving up the queen to break White's control of e6 is insufficient as White will come out a pawn ahead: 22...Qxe6 23.Qxe6 Rxe6 24.Rxe6 Nc6 25.Nxd5.
23.Nxd5
White is in firm control. His pieces are well centralized while Black's are not. Both of Black's knights are on the rim, while his e8-rook is stuck behind the e7-pawn.
23...Ng4 24.Qd2
White attacks the unprotected knight on a5.
24...Nf6?
Black attempts to make an indirect trade of the a5-knight for White's d5-knight. He should play 24...Nc4 instead, after which White's advantage would not be so great.
25.Qxa5 b6 (D)
